Optimal Control of Nonlinear PDE Systems
Using Reduced Order Modeling and Neural
Networks
Nikolaj Takata Mücke
Kongens Lyngby 2019
==PAGE:2==
Technical University of Denmark

Richard Petersens Plads, building 303b,
2800 Kongens Lyngby, Denmark

==PAGE:3==
Summary
The goal of the thesis is to assess the potential of replacing traditional elements of scientiﬁc comput-
ing in nonlinear dymamic PDE-constrained optimization with reduced order models based on proper
orthogonal decomposition and artiﬁcial neural networks.
The theory is divided into three main parts:
• PDE-Constrained Optimization.
• Reduced Basis Methods.
• Artiﬁcial Neural Networks.
Regarding PDE-constrained optimization, the general setup and optimality conditions are presented
and an all-at-once SQP method is discussed. For the reduced basis theory there will be special focus
on a Galerkin approach for the model formulation and the proper orthogonal decomposition (POD)
for basis generation. The combination of the SQP and the POD method culminates in the POD-SQP
method. Lastly, we will present the structure of neural networks and discuss their approximation
properties as well as how to train them. This all leads to the method we call the POD-SQP-NN
method which combines techniques from the three areas in order to solve nonlinear optimal control
problems eﬃciently.
The presentation of results is divided into two parts. Firstly, we present performance results of the
POD-Galerkin method applied to various time dependent PDE problems, including a 2D heat equation
with source term, a linear advection-diﬀusion-reaction equation with Robin boundary conditions, and
the nonlinear viscous Burgers’ equation. In all cases we ﬁnd the POD-Galerkin approach to decrease
computation time without compromising the accuracy.
Secondly, we present how reduced models perform for solving nonlinear PDE-constrained optimization
of Burgers’ equation and a nonlinear diﬀusion-reaction equation with and without constraints on the
control function. In both cases we only get a very modest speed-up, if any, by using the POD-SQP
method and in some of the cases involving Burgers’ equation the method does not converge. Solving
the problem using the POD-SQP-NN method results in major computation time savings, but does
come with lower relative accuracy at around 10−2 and 10−3 compared to the full order models.

Preface
This master thesis was prepared at DTU Compute in fulﬁlment of the requirements for acquiring an
M.Sc. degree in Mathematical Modelling and Computation. The thesis has been carried out in dur-
ing the period from September 27th 2018 to January 27th 2019 with associate Professor Allan Peter
Engsig-Karup and associate Professor John Bagterp Jørgensen as main supervisors and Ph.D. student
Lasse Hjuler Christiansen as co-supervisor. The workload corresponds to 30 ECTS.
I am grateful to my three supervisors for the help needed throughout the writing process.
I would particularly like to express my gratitude to Lasse Hjuler Christiansen for proposing the project,
the weekly meetings, the much appreciate insight, and the help with the coding.
Furthermore, I would like to thank Professor Jan Hesthaven for inputs throughout the semester.
Lyngby, 25-January-2019
Nikolaj Takata Mücke

Chapter 1
Introduction
In countless applications, including industrial, ﬁnancial, engineering, and scientiﬁc, the need for op-
timization is imminent [QB03], [CB07], [LBE+14]. Whether it is regarding optimal shape design in
ﬂows [BLUU12], deriving optimal strategies for oil extraction [CCJ16], or medical imaging [MGDB18],
the optimization algorithms need to be fast and reliable. Therefore, there is a great interest in the
study and improvement of these methods.
Computational Bottleneck
With increasing advances in the various ﬁelds the mathematical models describing the phenomena of
interest are becoming more complicated resulting in longer computation times. In real-time processes
such as model predictive control (MPC) systems (see ﬁgure 1.1), the optimization needs to be almost
instantaneous to avoid bottlenecks. When the mathematical model is a partial diﬀerential equation
(PDE) fast black-box optimization algorithms are rarely suﬃcient to ensure the computational speed
needed [LBE+14], especially in the case of nonlinear PDEs. Therefore, specialized solvers are essential.
There are several approaches to build specialized solvers, e.g.:
• Preconditioned Iterative Methods [PS13].
• High-performance Computing (HPC) [BG05].
• Reduced Models [QMN15], [HRS+16].
Iterative methods and HPC increases eﬃciency without compromising accuracy while reduced models
aim to balance a trade-oﬀbetween speed and accuracy. It should be noted that the three approaches
are deﬁnitely not mutually exclusive. For example does preconditioned iterative methods often work
great with parallelization. However, in this thesis we will focus on reduced models in order to be
able to solve PDE-constrained optimization problems eﬃciently. A reduced model is a model that
approximates the full model suﬃciently well, which in turn should be faster to solve. These have
received much attention recently, but repeatedly show struggles when it comes to complex nonlinear
applications [Ubb17], [QMN15].
==PAGE:16==
2
Introduction
Figure 1.1: A model of a closed loop MPC system. The optimization step is highlighted since it often
ends up as the bottleneck when the mathematical model is a PDE. Source: [Chr19].
Data-driven Models
Many techniques within the ﬁeld of scientiﬁc computing have been developed in order to solve in-
creasingly complicated PDE based problems. These include ﬁnite element methods [WM17], ﬁnite
volume methods [QQ09], spectral methods [XCM+18] , etc. However, common for all of them is the
curse of dimensionality. In general these methods do not scale very well. This motivates the need for
alternative approaches [BEKM16].
The ﬁrst step towards a reduced model is the well-known reduced basis methods [HRS+16], [QMN15].
Here, we utilize the before mentioned methods to generate solutions and use those to identify a more
eﬃcient basis in order to reduce the dimension of the problem.
Another approach, inspired by the recent success of machine learning [Tra14] and building upon the
reduced basis methods, are algorithms that combine the traditional approaches with methods from
e.g. deep learning [Ubb17], [HU18], [SMA18]. With this approach the central idea is to interpolate
the solution space and thereby get an equation free surrogate model.
In contrast to the traditional methods, the reduced basis and the deep learning methods require data
to train the models.
Main Objective
With all this in mind, the goal of the thesis is to study these new approaches. The main objective of
the thesis can thus be stated as follows:
Assess the potential of using reduced order models and artiﬁcial neural networks as re-
placements for traditional scientiﬁc computing elements in nonlinear PDE-constrained
optimization.
==PAGE:17==
3
Approach
To be able to address this objective we need to have an understanding of the main components involved.
These are
• PDE-constrained Optimization.
• Reduced Order Models.
• Artiﬁcial Neural Networks.
Throughout the thesis there will primarily be focus on the methods and implementations. Hence, a
great amount of time will be spent on deriving and explaining the relevant algorithms and how to
implement them. This, in turns, means that there will be less focus on the fundamental theory and
proofs. It is, of course, necessary to present some theory in order to understand the methods at hand.
To showcase how well the methods perform we resort to numerical case studies and present vari-
ous performance measures such as convergence and computation time. Everything is implemented in
the open-source scripting language Python with heavy reliance on the library NumPy. Furthermore,
the open-source frameworks FEniCS [ABH+15] and PyTorch [PGC+17] will be utilized for ﬁnite el-
ements and neural networks respectively. See Appendix A for an outline of the use of FEniCS and
Appendix B for an outline of the use of PyTorch.
Thesis Structure
• Preliminary Theory. In this chapter we present the necessary mathematical theory. Approx-
imately half the chapter deals with functional analysis, brieﬂy covering the topics of function
spaces, derivatives in Banach spaces, projections and approximations. The second half of the
chapter concerns full order numerical methods for solving PDEs. The focus will speciﬁcally be
on the Galerkin ﬁnite element method. Lastly the framework FEniCS is presented.
The purpose of this chapter is to lay the foundation of concepts and methods that will be utilized
and built upon later in the thesis.
• Optimial Control of PDEs. This chapter presents the PDE-constrained optimization frame-
work which we will be dealing with later.
The focus is on presenting the central problems
and methods to solve them. We will speciﬁcally present the formal Lagrange method and the
sequential quadratic programming approach.
In the end of this chapter we will discuss the
computational challenges of such problems and why there is a need for reduced order models.
• Reduced Basis Methods for PDEs. This chapter makes up the largest part of the thesis
and deals with the reduced basis approach to derive reduced order models. The general ideas
will be presented together with the concepts of oﬄine and online phases. A special focus will
be on the proper orthogonal decomposition as a mean to derive reduced bases. To this end we
present the POD-SQP method as mean to solve PDE-constrained problems using reduced bases.
• Artiﬁcial Neural Networks. This chapter is devoted to the theory of artiﬁcial neural net-
works. Here, we will start by presenting the structure of neural networks. From here, we will
move on to presenting the approximation properties. Thereafter we present methods to train and
discuss the possible diﬃculties associated with the training process. Lastly we will connect the
dots by showing how PDE-constrained optimization, reduced basis methods and neural networks
can be used together in a method we call the POD-SQP-NN method.
• Test Cases. The test cases are divided into two chapters:
==PAGE:18==
4
Introduction
– Time Dependent PDEs. This chapter will be used to show the strengths and weaknesses
of the reduced basis methods. We will show various measures to discuss the eﬃciency.
– Optimal Control of Nonlinear PDEs. In this chapter we showcase how the POD-SQP
and SQP-POD-NN methods are applied and how well they perform. We will investigate two
nonlinear test cases: i) A nonlinear reaction-diﬀusion equation and ii) Burgers’ equation.
• Conclusions and Future Work. Lastly we will conclude the thesis and propose future work
on the topic.
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 2
Preliminary Theory
2.1
Functional Analysis
We begin by presenting the relevant functional analysis theory. Functional analysis is, of course, a
huge topic and can easily make up an entire thesis by itself. In this thesis we restrict ourselves to
only include the relevant concepts and statements such as function spaces, derivatives, projections
and approximations. All of which are relevant either for the computation of the numerical solutions,
optimal control, reduced basis methods or neural networks.
2.1.1
Function Spaces
We will here give a short introduction to the function spaces relevant for this thesis. These include
the spaces of continuous functions, the Lp-spaces and W k,p-spaces. The aim is not to get a thorough
understanding of the spaces and their properties, but rather to ensure a common understanding of
what is meant when these are referenced.

2.1.2
Gâteuax and Fréchet Derivatives
In the study of PDE-constrained optimization we need to deﬁne the notions of derivatives in Banach
spaces. In short, these notions are generalizations of the traditional derivative of functions, to opera-
tors.
We start by deﬁning the Gâteaux derivative [Trö10]:
Deﬁnition 2.7 (Gâteaux Derivative). Let F be an operator on an open subset, X, of a Banach space,
U, into a Banach space, Y . Then we say that F is Gâteaux diﬀerentiable at x ∈X if there is a
bounded and linear operator, DxF(x) : X →Y , such that
lim
t→0
F(x + th) −F(x)
t
= DxF(x)h,
uniformly for all h ∈X. The operator DxF(x) is called the Gâteaux derivative of F at x.
Remark: We often call DxF(x)h the directional derivative or the ﬁrst variation at x in the direction
of h [Trö10].
Remark: We will often denote Fx(x) := DxF(x).
The Gâteaux derivative can be considered a generalization of the traditional derivative of functions to
a derivative of operators on Banach spaces. It is worth spending some time to exemplify some special
cases of the derivative, due to their frequent appearance.
==PAGE:22==
8
Preliminary Theory
Example 2.1 (Gâteuax Derivative of Special Cases of Operators).
(i) Linear Operator. Let A : U →V be a linear operator. Then we have, for x, h ∈U,
DxA(x)h = lim
t→0
A(x + th) −Ax
t
= lim
t→0
Ax + tAh −Ax
t
= lim
t→0
tAh
t
= Ah.
Therefore, the directional derivative of a linear operator at x in the direction h is simply the
operator applied on the direction regardless of x.
(ii) Inner Product. Let ⟨·, ·⟩H be the inner product in a Hilbert space H and deﬁne F(x, y) =
⟨x, y⟩H. Then
DxF(x, y)h = lim
t→0
F(x + th, y) −F(x, y)
t
= lim
t→0
⟨x + th, y⟩H −⟨x, y⟩H
t
= lim

⟨x, y⟩H + t ⟨h, y⟩H −⟨x, y⟩H
t
= ⟨h, y⟩H .
This result is not surprising, but rather a special case of (i), since a linear operator can be
interpreted as an inner product due to Riesz representation theorem [Chr10].
(iii) Square of Norm in a Hilbert Space. Let ||·||H denote the norm in a Hilbert space, H, and
Fx = ||x||2

The next concept to introduce is the second Gâteaux derivative of an operator. Take F : U × U →V .
Then the second Gâteaux derivative can be considered a bilinear map from U × U to V ,
DxyF(x, y)(h, k) := Dx(DyF(x, y)h)k,
which is ﬁrst taking the derivative with respect to x in the direction of h and then the derivative with
respect to y in the direction of k.
As expected, the Gâteaux derivative has many of the same properties as the common derivative in
Euclidean spaces, such as the chain rule:
Theorem 2.8 (Chain Rule). Let F : U →V and G : V →Z be, not necessarily linear, operators,
and E = G ◦F. Then
DxE(x) = DF (x)(G(F(x))) ◦DxF(x)
An important example in PDE-constrained optimization of the above theorem in action is the following.
==PAGE:23==
2.1 Functional Analysis
9
Example 2.2. Deﬁne H : U →R, x 7→⟨x, v⟩, L : U →U a nonlinear operator, and E = H ◦L.
Then, by virtue of the chain rule and example 2.1, we have for x ∈U
DxE(x)h = (DL(x)H(L(x)) ◦DxL(x))h = H (DxL(x)h) = ⟨DxL(x)h, v⟩
(2.14)
Next, we have the Fréchet derivative [Trö10]:
Deﬁnition 2.9. A map, F : X ⊂U →Y is said to be Fréchet diﬀerentiable at u ∈X if there exists a
linear and bounded operator A : U →Y and a mapping r(u, ·) : U →Y with the following properties:
For all h ∈U, such that u + h ∈U, we have
F(u + h) = F(u) + Ah + r(u, h),
(2.15)
where the remainder, r satisﬁes
||r(u, h)||V
||h||U
→0,
||h||U →0.
(2.16)
The operator A is then called the Fréchet derivative of F at u, and we write F ′(u). If A is Fréchet
diﬀerentiable in every point in U we say that F is Fréchet diﬀerentiable in U.
It is slightly more restrictive to require Fréchet diﬀerentiability than Gâteaux diﬀerentiability. How-
ever, one can show that if a map is Fréchet diﬀerentiable then the Fréchet derivative coincides with
the Gâteaux derivative. Since the Gâteaux derivative is easier to deal with, we, in general, simply
compute the Gâteaux derivative, even though we state it as the Fréchet derivative.
These concepts of derivatives in Banach spaces are crucial for the derivation of the optimality condi-
tions in PDE-constrained optimization. This is because, heuristically speaking, ﬁnding a minimum of
a functional is "equivalent" to computing a minimum of a function on Rn where one would compute
the gradient and determine where it vanishes. These concepts are quite transferable to Banach spaces
(in idea, but not in the rigorous formulations!).
2.1.3
Projections
For a Hilbert space V and a closed subspace W a projection is a representation of an element x ∈V
as an element w ∈W. In the case of x ∈W the two elements coincide. However, if this is not the
case, one is interested in ﬁnding the element in W that minimizes ||x −w||V . To this end we start by
deﬁning an orthogonal projection [QMN15]:
Deﬁnition 2.10. Let V be a Hilbert space W ⊂V a closed subspace. Then the orthogonal projection,
ΠW : V →W, onto W is the unique operator that satisﬁes
⟨x −ΠW x, w⟩V = 0,
∀w ∈W.
(2.17)
If W = span{wi}Nw
i=1, one can show that the orthogonal projection operator is given by [Jen15]
ΠW v =
Nw
X
i=1
⟨v, wi⟩V wi.
(2.18)
Among all possible projections onto a subspace, the orthogonal projection has a special signiﬁcance
due to the following theorem [QMN15]:
==PAGE:24==
10
Preliminary Theory
Theorem 2.11 (Orthogonal Projection Theorem). Let V be a Hilbert space, W a closed subspace of
V . Then for any vector x ∈V there exists a unique element ΠW x such that
||x −ΠW x||V = inf
w∈W ||x −w||V ,
(2.19)
where ΠW : V →W is the orthogonal projection of V onto W.
Because of theorem 2.11 and the fact that we have a clear expression for the orthogonal projection in
(2.18), one would almost always use the orthogonal projection when dealing with projection problems.
Theorem 2.11 will later prove to be very important for the reduced basis theory. This is due to the
fact that the whole theory of reduced basis methods is about representing a Hilbert spaces by a lower
dimensional subspace.
2.1.4
Approximations
In this section we will brieﬂy touch upon the concepts of denseness. The concept of denseness is
relying on a metric, d, in a given metric space, (V, d). Since we are only dealing with normed spaces,
we will, by a metric, d(x, y), always mean the diﬀerence in the norm of the space. That is,
d(x, y) = ||x −y|| .
(2.20)
The concept of denseness can then be summarized in the following deﬁnition [Chr10]
Deﬁnition 2.12. Let V be a normed space and W a subset of V . We then say that W is dense in V
if for every x ∈V there is a sequence {wi}∞
i=1 ⊂W, such that
wi →x,
for
i →∞.
(2.21)
Or in other words: Given x ∈V and ϵ > 0 there is a w ∈W such that
||w −x||V < ϵ.
(2.22)
In short, a subset is dense in a space if all elements in the larger space can be approximated arbitrarily
well by an element in the subset. To be precise one would have to state what norm one is referring
to when stating that a subset is dense in a space. However, if nothing else is explicitly stated, it is
implicitly meant that the norm from the given space is what makes up the metric.
Example 2.3. A much celebrated example of a subset being dense is the Stone-Weierstrass Theorem
[Chr10]. This states the set of all polynomials are dense in the space of continuous function on a
closed interval, i.e. C([a, b]). This means that given f ∈C([a, b]) and ϵ > 0 there is a polynomial P
of some degree, that satisﬁes
||f −P|| = max
x∈[a,b] |f(x) −P(x)| < ϵ.
The concept of denseness is a very convenient way of stating the approximation properties of a class
of functions. Later, we will discuss such properties for neural networks where this terminology will be
used extensively.
2.2
High-Fidelity Methods
We mentioned the overall strategy in the introduction.
First we have to compute so called high-
ﬁdelity solutions and then we will generate a reduced model based on these. In order to generate these
==PAGE:25==
2.2 High-Fidelity Methods
11
solutions we have to understand the basic ideas and principles of computing full order solutions. In
this section we will show how to deal with PDEs by presenting the Galerkin method. Then we move on
to show how to deal with dynamic PDEs. After this we show the Newton approach to solve nonlinear
problems and lastly we introduce the ﬁnite element framework FEniCS.
2.2.1
Galerkin Finite Element Method
The theory of scientiﬁc computing is well developed and there are many advanced techniques to solve
PDEs computationally eﬃcient.
These include spectral methods [Kop09], Discontinuous Galerkin
methods [HW07], Isogeometric methods [CHB09], etc. Each with their advantages and disadvantages
which make some methods suitable for some problems but not for other. Since we will ensure com-
putational eﬃciency through reduced basis methods, we will simply present the "standard" Galerkin
Finite Element method here [EG13]. This method, despite its simplicity, is very famous due to its
versatility. It works on (almost) all domains and it is fairly easy to implement and get descent results.
In general when discretizing a problem, we seek to minimize the residual between the true solution
and a discrete one. For the representation of the discrete solution we use an approximation, which can
either be i) modal or ii) nodal. In the modal approach, one computes the coeﬃcients of some ﬁnite
number of basis functions. Here, the computed values have no immediate physical interpretation.
In the nodal approach, however, one computes a solution which can be directly interpreted. More
precisely, one computes values of the solution on a discrete domain [Kop09]. We will, in this report,
focus on the modal approach.
The solution of a given PDE in some Hilbert space, V , can be represented by a linear combination of
the bases,
y(x) =
∞
X
i=1
ˆyiφi(x).
(2.23)
In the ﬁnite element approach, we seek to compute a solution as a truncated series:
yδ(x) =
Nδ
X
i=1
ˆyiϕi(x),
(2.24)
where ˆyi ∈R are the generalized Fourier coeﬃcients, i.e. the unknowns to be computed, ϕi : Ω→R
are some chosen basis functions and Nδ ≥1 is the dimension of the ﬁnite-dimensional subspace. The
diﬀerence between eq. (2.23) and eq. (2.24) is an error term. We denote the ﬁnite dimensional sub-
space Vδ = span {ϕi}Nδ
i=1. In the case of conforming ﬁnite elements methods we have Vδ ⊂V .
We will showcase the idea of the Galerkin method on the Poisson equation:

form, also called the variational form by multiplying by a test function, ϕ ∈H1(Ω), and integrating
over the domain

For the Galerkin method the test function is from the same function space as the solution (also called
trial) function. From hereon, one would have to choose a ﬁnite dimensional subspace, Vδ, often denoted
a ﬁnite element space. To this end, one would ﬁrst have to discretize the domain by introducing a
triangulation. That is, dividing the domain into a ﬁnite number of triangles or, in the one dimensional
case, simply intervals. The nodes of this triangulation will then make up a set, P, consisting of a
ﬁnite number of points.
The basis functions are now deﬁned based on the triangulation.
In this
thesis we will simply consider ﬁrst order, or simply linear, Lagrange ﬁnite element functions, which
is a collection of so called hat functions [EK09]. A characteristic of the ﬁnite element method is that
the element functions have compact support on subdomains, which makes it quite versatile when it
comes to solving problems on complicated domains. The number of discrete points are the same as the
number of basis functions, Nδ. Furthermore, in the case of linear ﬁnite elements the basis functions
have the cardinal property [EK09]:
ϕj(xi) = δij =
(
1
for i = j
0
else

(2.29)
Inserting (2.24) in (2.28) and choosing the test function to be ϕj, we get
a

Introducing the vectors
y = (ˆy1, . . . , ˆyNδ),

(2.30)
and the matrix A ∈RNδ×Nδ, with

we can formulate the linear system to be solved:
Ay = f.
(2.32)
If one was to use higher order elements more points would be necessary to ensure that the integrals
a (ϕi, ϕj) would be exact, or at least precise enough according to some threshold, but in the linear case
Nδ is enough to ensure exact integration. It can, furthermore, be shown that linear ﬁnite elements
give a second order approximation [EK09].

This was only a very brief summary of the Galerkin ﬁnite element method. Issues regarding when a
ﬁnite element space is capable of approximating the inﬁnite dimensional solution are of course valid
concerns. In these cases one would have to look at the bilinear form a(·, ·) and the functional f to
determine error estimates of the ﬁnite element solution. Here is a brief summary of the relevant results
[QMN15]:
==PAGE:27==
2.2 High-Fidelity Methods
13
1. If a(·, ·) is strongly coercive, bilinear, and continuous and f is bounded and linear, then error
estimates can be obtained from Ceas Lemma [QMN15].
2. If a(·, ·) is weakly coercive, bilinear, and continuous and f is bounded and linear, then error
estimates can be obtained from Babuskas theorem [QMN15]. This is for example the case when
the test function space and the solution function space do not coincide. In this case we call the
resulting method a Petrov-Galerkin method [QMN15].
3. If a(·, ·) denotes a saddle-point problem, often written A((·, ·), (·, ·)), one would use Brezzis
theorem [QMN15]. These kind of problems often occur in PDE-constrained optimization. See
for example the derivation is section 3.2.3.
The above, very brief overview, is only for linear problems.
2.2.2
Time Dependent Problems
There are more than one way to deal with time-dependent PDEs. One could use space-time elements
where both time and space are approximated by element functions, as is done in [LSW16]. However,
in this report we only discretize in space using the basis functions and discretize in time using a ﬁnite
diﬀerence approach. This "moves" the time-dependency to the coeﬃcients:
y(x, t) =
Nδ
X
i=1
ˆyi(t)ϕi(x).
(2.34)
The kind of problems under considerations are the following
⟨∂ty(t), ϕ⟩L2 + a(y(t), ϕ) = f(ϕ),
∀ϕ ∈H1(Ω), ∀t ∈[0, T].
(2.35)
Here, we have skipped to the weak formulation. We will seek solutions in the space W 1,1
2
(Q), where
Q = [0, T] × Ωis a space-time cylinder. The strategy for solving these is to ﬁrst derive a spatially
ﬁnite dimensional version of (2.35) by inserting (2.34) and follow the procedure presented earlier:
M ˙y(t) + Ay(t) = f(t),
(2.36)
where Mij = ⟨ϕi, ϕj⟩. M is often called the mass matrix. For the discretization in time we introduce
a ﬁnite diﬀerence discretization. Hence, we divide the time interval into K subintervals of length
∆t = T/K and deﬁne tk = k∆t, for 0 ≤k ≤K. One would often then either use a backward or a
forward diﬀerence for the time derivative, called the implicit and explicit Euler method respectively
[LeV98]. But for, especially, nonlinear problems, using an Euler scheme is often not the best approach.
Even though the implicit Euler method is unconditionally stable there exists methods that allow you
take larger steps and still keep the same accuracy. To this end, we make use of a generalization of the
Euler schemes: The θ-Scheme. In the θ-scheme one averages over the current and previous time step
and thereby makes it a combination of an explicit and implicit scheme. To arrive at this scheme, we
ﬁrstly introduce
Fk(y(tk)) = Ay(tk) −f(tk).
(2.37)
Then we get the scheme, to take a time step forward, to be
M 1
∆t(yk −yk−1) + θFk(yk) + (1 −θ)Fk−1(yk−1) = 0.
(2.38)
Hence, in each time step the following system is solved for yk = y(tk) to advance in time:
 1
∆tM + θA

yk =
 1
δtM −(1 −θ)A

yk−1(µ) + θfk + (1 −θ)fk−1.
(2.39)
θ ∈[0, 1] is the parameter determining how implicit/explicit the scheme is. For θ = 1 the scheme
equals the implicit Euler and is thereby fully implicit. Similarly for θ = 0 the scheme is the explicit
Euler and thereby fully explicit.
==PAGE:28==
14
Preliminary Theory
2.2.3
Nonlinear Problems
There are many approaches to solve nonlinear PDEs, but arguably one of the most common and
celebrated methods is the Newton method, due to it being a very versatile method exhibiting second
order convergence [QMN15].
For time dependent problems, however, methods such as explicit schemes are often used due to it’s
simplicity and easy implementation, even though stability is a concern to address in such cases.
The general nonlinear problem is of the form
G(y) = 0,
in
Ω.
(2.40)
The choice of solution space for y is more troublesome than for the linear case since it depends on the
operator G. For this section we will simply denote the solution space V . In the time-dependent case
one often writes
∂ty + N(y) = 0,
(2.41)
where N : V →R is a nonlinear operator. Hence, the nonlinearity is on the spatial derivatives. By
deﬁning
G(y) = ∂ty + N(y),
(2.42)
one simply needs to solve a problem on the form (2.40), and after a time discretization, the solution
of that problem will correspond to taking one step forward in time.
To solve (2.40) numerically it is necessary to linearize. This can be done at many stages: (i) At
the algebraic level. That is, ﬁrst derive a nonlinear discrete problem and then linearize. (ii) At the
continuous level. Here, one linearizes the continuous inﬁnite dimensional problem and arrive at a
linear PDE, which can then be solved by methods presented earlier. We will continue with approach
(ii).
Using Newtons method one solve the nonlinear problem by solving a series of linear problems for
δy = yn+1 −yn, of the form
DyG(yn)δy = −G(yn),
(2.43)
where DyG(yn) is the Fréchet derivative at yn in the direction δy. The superscript denotes the Newton
iteration and must not be confused with the previous notation for time step. Then one updates the
solution by
yn+1 = yn + δy.
(2.44)
Or, by inserting δy = yn+1 −yn, one solves the following problem directly for yn+1:
DyG(yn)yn+1 = −G(yn) −DyG(yn)yn.
(2.45)
Thus, one needs to choose an initial guess for the solution and then iterate until a satisfactory solution
is obtained according to some measure. A termination criteria could, for example, be to stop when
||δy|| < ϵ.
Each of the problems (2.43) can be solved using the linear ﬁnite element methods presented above.
If G fulﬁlls certain smoothness criteria, one can show that the Newton method converges towards
the true solution, y∗, and it exhibits second order convergence [QMN15], which means that for some
C < 1, we have:
yn+1 −y∗
V ≤C ||yn −y∗||2
V .
(2.46)
The convergence is, however, dependent on an initial choice to be suﬃciently close to the true solution.
There are unfortunately no general rule for choosing such an initial guess.
In section 3.2.3 we go through the derivation of the Newton method for a nonlinear saddle-point
problem, which showcases the method.
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 3
Optimal Control of Partial Diﬀerential
Equations
In this chapter we will outline the fundamental theory, concepts, theorems, and numerical methods
for optimal control of PDEs. This subject is vast and it would be impossible to cover it all in a single
chapter. However, we will present the necessary elements for discussing and solving quadratic control
problems constrained by a special class of nonlinear PDEs.
We will start by presenting the general set-up and then move on to discussing how to arrive at an
optimality system to be solved. Lastly, we will present numerical methods, for solving such problems,
based on the theoretical results.
3.1
PDE-Constrained Optimization
The PDE-condtrained optimization problem of interest is of the form:
min
u∈Uad,y∈V J(y, u) :=
Z
Q
|y(x, t) −yd(x, t)|2 dQ + β
2
Z
Q
|u(x, t)|2 dQ
(3.1a)
s.t.
∂ty −∆y + N(y) = f + u,
in
Q
(3.1b)
y(x, t) = 0,
on
Σ
(3.1c)
y(x, 0) = y0,
in
Ω
(3.1d)
where y ∈V is the state of the system, u ∈Uad is the control, yd ∈L∞(Q) is the desired state of the
system, and Q = Ω×I is the space-time domain. N : Q →Q is a nonlinear (or linear for that matter)
operator. J : V × Uad →R is called the objective functional and (3.1b)-(3.1d) are the constraints. For
now V is an undeﬁned function space. Furtermore, the space Uad is the set of admissible controls. We
will start by discussing the unconstrained distributed control problem. Here, there are no constraints
on the control function and we therefore set Uad = L∞(Q). Later in the chapter we introduce a
constrained case.
Existence and Uniqueness
To discuss existence and uniqueness of eq. (3.1) we need to choose a speciﬁc space V and discuss
assumptions on the operator N. The choice of space for the state, y, is chosen to be V = W(0, T) ∩
==PAGE:30==
16
Optimal Control of Partial Diﬀerential Equations
C( ¯Q). For the existence we have the following result [Trö10]:
Theorem 3.1 (Existence). Suppose
1. Ω⊂RN is a bounded Lipschitz domain.
2. The function
N = N(x, t, y) : Q × R →R,
is measurable with respect to (x, t) for all y ∈R, and is twice diﬀerentiable with respect to y for
almost all (x, t).
3. We have
Ny(x, t, y) ≥0
for almost every (x, t) ∈Q.
4. The initial condition y0 ∈C(¯Ω).
5. The bounds ua, ub ∈L∞(Q) and ua(x, t) ≤ub(x, t) for almost every (x, t) ∈Q.
Then (3.1) has at least one optimal control u∗with associated state y∗= y∗(u∗)
The assumptions in theorem 3.1 is a restating of assumptions 5.6 in [Trö10] where a slightly more
general case is considered.
The discussion of uniqueness is more involved and requires a so called control-to-state operator which
maps the controls to the state. In this thesis we will not go into deeper details about this issue and
simply assume uniqueness or refer to results from various sources.
Optimality Conditions
In short, the optimality conditions are conditions to be satisﬁed in order to ensure that an optimal
solution has been found.
There are generally two types of optimality conditions: (i) First order
conditions and (ii) second order conditions.
The ﬁrst order conditions involves the ﬁrst order derivative of the problem and is a necessary condition.
In the unconstrained case it simply states that for a functional, J : V →R, z∗is a local minimum of
J if and only if [HPUU08]
DzJ(z∗) = 0.
This is quite similar to minimizing a function on RN where one needs the gradient to vanish. For the
constrained case it is quite a bit more complicated and we will discuss one approach to derive ﬁrst
order conditions in the coming section.
The second order conditions, on the other hand, involve the second variation and is a suﬃcient
condition [Trö10]. Including the second order variations is often computationally quite expensive.
Therefore, it is quite common to only use the ﬁrst order conditions. We will, in this thesis, follow that
approach and only make use of the ﬁrst order optimality conditions to derive an optimality system.
==PAGE:31==
3.1 PDE-Constrained Optimization
17
3.1.1
The Formal Lagrange Method
The optimality conditions can be derived in more than one way. In [Trö10] they are analyzed rigorously
by utilizing the control-to-state operator to derive variational inequalities, which are then simpliﬁed
by introducing the adjoint state. This approach is very suitable for proving the optimality conditions
for eq. (3.1). However, another approach, much more suitable for actually deriving them, is the for-
mal Lagrange method. It is quite close to the methods often used in ﬁnite dimensional optimization
presented in books such as [NW06]. However, due to the inﬁnite dimensional setting, the rigorous
analysis is much more delicate. Problems such as choosing the right spaces can be very diﬃcult and
time consuming, but it is very convenient in a more applied setting [Trö10]. We will, during the
presentation of the method, point out where assumptions are being made, which is the reason for the
inclusion of the term "formal".
We start by collecting eq. (3.1b)-(3.1d) into the general form
e(y, u) = 0,
(3.2)
for convenience. Where e : V × Uad →R, contains the state equation, including boundary and intial
conditions and V = W(0, T) ∩C( ¯Q). Next, we deﬁne the Lagrangian, L : V × Uad × V ∗→R, for the
problem (3.1) [Trö10]:
L(y, u, p) := J(y, u) +
Z
Q
e(y, u)p dQ,
(3.3)
where p ∈V ∗. We call p the Lagrange multiplier or adjoint state. Note that the integral corresponds
to the dual pairing between V and it’s dual. Hence, we can write
L(y, u, p) = J(y, u) + ⟨e(y, u), p⟩V ∗,V .
(3.4)
In (3.4) the term ∆y occurs. Since we are looking for solutions y ∈V , i.e. only once diﬀerentiable in
space, this term does not necessarily make sense. Hence, we only consider it formally.
First Order Optimality Conditions
The ﬁrst order optimal conditions, in terms of the Lagrangian, are often denoted the KKT conditions,
named after Karush, Kuhn and Tucker [BV04].
Deﬁnition 3.2 (KKT Conditions). A triple (y∗, u∗, p∗) is satisfying the KKT conditions if
DyL(y∗, u∗, p∗)η = 0,
∀η ∈V,
(3.5a)
DuL(y∗, u∗, p∗)ζ = 0,
∀ζ ∈Uad,
(3.5b)
DpL(y∗, u∗, p∗)ξ = 0,
∀ξ ∈V ∗.
(3.5c)
In other words, (y∗, u∗, p∗) is a stationary point of the Lagrangian.
Eq. (3.5) is often called the KKT-system. The reason for introducing the KKT conditions is the
following.
Theorem 3.3. (y∗, u∗, p∗) solves (3.1) if and only if it is a stationary point of the corresponding
Lagrangian.
This result is stated in diﬀerent ways in e.g. [Trö10] and [NW07], but it boils down to the statement
above. Equipped with theorem 3.3, we can solve (3.1) by solving the KKT-system.

Optimal Control of Partial Diﬀerential Equations

3.2 Numerical Methods
19
It is important to note that the state equation is evolving forward in time since it has an initial
condition, while the adjoint state is evolving backward in time due to the terminal condition.
3.2
Numerical Methods
When solving PDE-constrained optimal control problems numerically, one needs to transform the
continuous setting into a discrete one. As shown above, we have to solve a system of nonlinear time-
dependent PDEs. We will utilize the methods from section 2.2, but with some additional considerations
which we will show along the way. These include the troubles of dealing with systems of PDEs and
having one equation running forward in time and one running backwards.
3.2.1
Optimize then Discretize vs. Discretize then Optimize
There are, in general, two fundamental approaches to solving optimal control problems such as eq.
(3.1) numerically: i) Optimize-then-discretize (OD) and ii) Discretize-then-optimize (DO) [YK14].
Discretize-then-optimize
As the name suggests, when using the DO approach one starts by discretizing the functional J(y, u) and
the constraints using some ﬁnite element discretization and thereafter derive the optimality conditions.
Inserting (2.34) in (3.1) we get the following discrete problem
min
u,y J(y, u) =
Z T
0
1
2(y −yd)T M(y −yd) dt + β
2
Z T
0
uT Mu dt,
(3.10a)
s.t.
M ˙y + Ky + N(y(t)) −Mu = f(t),
(3.10b)
My(0) = My0.
(3.10c)
Note that we jumped straight to the spatially discrete weak form of the state equation. We skip the
discussion on the time discretization for now. From hereon, one can derive the optimality conditions
using the discrete Lagrangian. It is important to notice that eq. (3.10) is a ﬁnite dimensional algebraic
system once the time discretization has been done. We will not pursue the approach further in this
thesis.
Optimize-then-discretize
In contrast to the DO approach, solving eq. (3.1) numerically using the OD strategy, entails ﬁrst
deriving the optimality conditions on the continuous level and then discretize the resulting optimality
system. We have already derived the optimality system on the continuous level in the previous section.
Hence, we need to discretize eq. (3.9) and solve the resulting system of equations. This will be done
step by step in a coming section.
3.2.2
Gradient Methods
Solving the optimal control problem is not a trivial task, regardless of whether one is considering the
OD or the DO approach. For the OD approach one runs into eq. (3.9) which is a system of coupled
==PAGE:34==
20
Optimal Control of Partial Diﬀerential Equations
nonlinear PDEs, whereas one is forward in time and the other backward in time. There are several
ways to handle this problem. One way is gradient based methods where one considers the reduced cost
functional, J(y(u), u) = J(u), and compute the stationary point, i.e. ﬁnd u∗, such that
DuJ(u∗)(u) = 0.
(3.11)
This method is an iterative multiple shooting scheme. We will not go into great details, but merely
present the algorithm as a motivation for other approaches. Instead we refer to [Trö10] for a more
detailed presentation. It is basically a gradient descent method and consists of ﬁve steps which are
repeated until a satisfying solution is achieved:
1. Compute the state yn = yn(un) by solving (3.8a) - (3.8c).
2. Compute the adjoint state, pn by solving (3.8d) - (3.8f). Note that this is a linear problem since
yn is computed in the previous step.
3. Compute descent direction, hn:
hn = −βpn.
(3.12)
4. Compute the step size, sn, from
min
s>0 F(un + shn).
(3.13)
5. Compute the new control un+1 by
un+1 = un + snhn,
(3.14)
and go to step 1.
The algorithm is terminated when the step size or the increment,
L(yn+1, un+1, pn+1) −L(yn, un, pn),
is suﬃciently small according to some chosen measure and tolerance.
Remark: Step 4 can be omitted without losing convergence, by choosing the step size suﬃciently
small [Trö10].
This method is fairly easy to implement, but is only exhibiting ﬁrst order convergence and is in-
credibly expensive, due to the fact that one has solve a nonlinear time dependent PDE in step 1, a
linear time dependent PDE in step 2 and compute a satisfying step size in step 4. Even though step
4 can be omitted, it will often result in slow convergence due to too small steps. Because of these
unpleasant properties, one often resorts to other schemes.
However, it should be noted that this approach is a global method. By that, we mean no matter the
initial guess we will arrive at at least a local minimum. In the case of convexity we will arrive at the
global minimum.
3.2.3
Sequential Quadratic Programming
In step 1 of the gradient method one has to solve a nonlinear PDE. This is often done by a Newton
method, which motivates the Sequential Quadratic Programming (SQP) approach. Instead of just
solving the state equation using a newton method, we consider the full KKT system at once.
==PAGE:35==
3.2 Numerical Methods
21
We start by reminding the reader that Newton’s method is used for solving nonlinear equations on
the form
F(x) = 0,
(3.15)
by updating a previous iterate xn according to the rule
DxF(x)(xn+1 −xn) = −F(xn),
(3.16)
starting at some initial guess x0. We then deﬁne w := (y, u, p) and introduce the notation D2L(w)
for the Hessian of the Lagrangian, and Ln = L(wn). Applying Newton’s method to the nonlinear
KKT-system now reveals the following system to solve in each iteration:
D2L(wn)(wn+1 −wn) = −DwL(wn).
(3.17)
By writing this out explicitly, we arrive at:


,
(3.18)
to solve in each iteration. Due to the fact that we linearized the KKT-system originating from the La-

solved for the next iterate, wn+1, or the increment δw = (wn+1−wn) and then update wn+1 = wn+δw.
Remark. Instead of looking at eq. (3.18) as the linearized KKT-system, one can interpret it as
the KKT-system for a linear-quadratic control problem. In this view we reach the solution by solving
a sequence of quadratic problems. Hence, the name sequential quadratic programming. In this case
the linear-quadratic problem is given as [Trö10]:
min
y,u
¯J(y, u) :=DwJ(wn)(δw) + 1
2DwwL(wn)(δw, δw),
(3.19a)
s.t.
∂ty −∆y + N(yn) + Ny(yn)(y −yn) = f + u,
in
Q,
(3.19b)
y(x, t) = 0,
on
Σ,
(3.19c)
y(x, 0) = y0,
on
Ω.
(3.19d)
Here, the solution (y, u) corresponds to the next iterate (yn+1, un+1).
Regarding the convergence of the SQP method we have the following [Trö10]:
Theorem 3.4. The SQP method exhibits second order convergence in th sense of L∞norm if the
initial guess is suﬃciently close to the optimal solution and the second order optimality conditions are
satisﬁed.
Which motivates using this approach instead of gradient methods. However, as stated in the theorem,
one needs to choose an initial guess suﬃciently close to the optimum. There are, in general, no clear
way to do this since it is highly dependent on the problem at hand. We will see later, that this actually
causes some problems. A good strategy is to combine gradient methods for the early stages of the
optimization and then switch to the SQP method after a certain number of iterations.
Eq. (3.18) is equivalent to linearizing the nonlinear ﬁrst order optimality system directly. In the
case of eq. (3.9), where u is eliminated, we have
F(y, p) = ∂ty −∆y + N(y) −1

Regarding the implementation of the SQP method, there are diﬃculties regarding the time evolu-
tion of the two equations. As mentioned, the state evolves forward in time and the adjoint state
backwards. This problem could be overcome by utilizing a multiple shooting method, such as gradient
methods as explained earlier. Alternatively, one can solve this system by using a so called Simultane-
ously Analysis and Design (SAND) also called a single shooting or an all-at-once method. With this
approach one solves for the state and adjoint at all time steps at the same time. This approach is
presented in [SW10] and [YK14].

when the initial guess is suﬃciently close to the optimum.
In practice, the semi-smooth Newton

the constraints are limiting the solution a lot. This will be shown later in the test cases.

Optimal Control of Partial Diﬀerential Equations
3.2.5
Computational Problems
(3.30) is very often a huge linear system and increases in size by a lot when only adding few extra
points in either space or time. In the case of complicated multiphysics problems many points are
necessary and often renders (3.30) infeasible to solve within a realistic time frame. This problem is
known as the curse of dimensionality and many methods traditionally used in scientiﬁc computing
suﬀers from this.
As mentioned in the introduction, there is a great interest in being able to solve PDE-constrained
optimization problems fast due to their applications in e.g. model predictive control. In these applica-
tions it is crucial that these systems can be solved almost in real-time or else they will not add much
value. Hence, it is worth spending time to design specialized methods to overcome the challenge.
There are, in general three approaches to minimize the computation time in solving (3.30):
1. High-Performance Computing.
2. Customized Solvers.
3. Reduced Models.
It should be noted that the three approaches are by no means mutually exclusive. In the ﬁrst approach
one makes use of the computer architecture to speed up computations. Methods such as parallelization
and GPU computing are very common in this regime. In the second approach, one would often make
use of eﬀective iterative solvers (possibly matrix-free), such as krylov subspace or multigrid methods.
Here, one often utilizes the sparse structure of the system to both save memory, but also to come up
with preconditioners to decrease the computation time signiﬁcantly. While there is much to be said
on these topics, we will not discuss these techniques further, but rather spend the rest of the thesis to
discuss the reduced model approach. Instead of trying to solve (3.30) eﬀectively, the goal is to reduce
the problem so much, that solving it is computationally fast. There are several ways to do this and
in this thesis we will explore a reduced basis approach using proper orthogonal decomposition as well
as an equation free surrogate model using neural networks, where one gets rid of the linear system
entirely.
==PAGE:41==
Chapter 4
Reduced Basis Methods for Partial
Diﬀerential Equations
As we have seen above, solving PDE-constrained optimization problems leads to very large linear
systems that need to be solved multiple times. Furthermore, in many applications one has to solve the
same optimization problem for varying parameter values, which puts increasing pressure on solving
the system fast. In this chapter we will explore a reduced basis (RB) approach to lower the dimension
of the problem. The main idea is to introduce an oﬄine phase where the reduced model is computed
and then this is solved in the online phase (see ﬁgure 4.1). We will go into further details on this later
in the chapter.
We will start by introducing the general idea behind reduced basis models.
Then we move onto
presenting a way to utilize the reduced model to solve PDEs.
In particular, we will present the
Galerkin projection method.
This will be shown for linear problems and then extended to time-
dependent, nonlinear and optimization problems. We ﬁnish by presenting a method, called the proper
orthogonal decomposition, to compute the reduced bases.
Figure 4.1:
The top bar visualizes the long computation time for each set of parameter values.
The lower one shows that by introducing a possibly long oﬄine phase, one can decrease each online
computation. Source: [Haa17].
==PAGE:42==
28
Reduced Basis Methods for Partial Diﬀerential Equations
4.1
Parametrized Partial Diﬀerential Equations
Before diving into the theory of RB methods, we will give an introduction to the general setting of the
problems at hand, namely parametrized partial diﬀerential equations. Parametrized PDEs are, as the
name suggests, PDEs depending on a set of parameters [QMN15]. We will denote these parameters
by µ. In general µ can represent physical or geometric properties. Physical properties refer to source
terms, boundary conditions, material properties, such as conductivity, etc. Geometric properties refer
to properties of the computational domain, such as size and shape. In the latter case one will consider
the domain as being parametrized by µ; Ω= Ω(µ). In this report, however, we will solely consider
physical parameters.
The parameters should be considered as an input-parameter vector, µ = (µ1, . . . , µP ). The set of all
possible input parameters is denoted P ⊂RP and is assumed to be a compact subset of RP [QMN15].
For a domain Ω⊂Rd and a suitable Hilbert space V
= V (Ω), we have the following general
parametrized problem in strong form: Given µ, ﬁnd y(µ) ∈V such that
G(y(µ);µ) = 0,
G : V × P →V ′,
(4.1)
and it’s corresponding variational (weak) form
g(y(µ), v;µ) = ⟨G(y(µ);µ), v⟩= 0,
∀v ∈V.
(4.2)
Note that for now we are only considering the stationary case. Later we will generalize this and include
a time dependence. g : V × V × P →R can be a linear, as well as nonlinear form, but note that in the
linear case we will almost always write the problem as follows: Given µ, ﬁnd y(µ) ∈V such that
a(y(µ), v;µ) = f(v;µ),
∀v ∈V,
(4.3)
where a : V × V × P →R is a parametrized bilinear form and f : V × P →R is a functional on V .
Existence and uniqueness of solutions to the parametrized linear variational problems are shown by
extending the theorems of Lax-Milgram for the coercive case, Babuskas for the non-coercive case,
and Brezzi for saddle-point problems [QMN15]. The general ideas are to show that for any given
input-parameters, µ ∈P, the conditions are satisﬁed.
4.2
The General Idea
The overall goal is, of course, to compute the solution of (4.2), y(µ). We call this solution the exact
solution. The set of all exact solutions, under variations of the parameters µ, is called the solution
manifold [HRS+16],
M = {y(µ) | µ ∈P} ⊂V.
(4.4)
In most cases the exact solution is not available, which means one has to approximate it. To this end,
we deﬁne a discrete ﬁnite dimensional subspace Vδ ⊂V and a corresponding discrete problem: Find
yδ(µ) ∈Vδ such that
g(yδ(µ), vδ;µ) = 0,
∀vδ ∈Vδ.
(4.5)
Which gives rise to a discrete version of the solution manifold:
Mδ = {yδ(µ) | µ ∈P} ⊂Vδ.
(4.6)
We will in general assume that the exact solution can be approximated arbitrarily well by a discrete
one. This means that ||y(µ) −yδ(µ)|| can be made arbitrarily small. Due to this notion, we will refer
==PAGE:43==
4.2 The General Idea
29
to yδ(µ) as the truth solution or the high-ﬁdelity solution. The usual choice of Vδ is based on ﬁnite
element, ﬁnite diﬀerence, or spectral methods and there are many ways, depending on the problem to
ensure that Vδ approximates V arbitrarily well. These considerations were brieﬂy discussed in section
2.2.
Even though we have established that the truth solution can approximate the exact solution arbi-
trarily well, there is a fundamental problem in the process of doing so. The computation of the truth
solution is highly dependent on Nδ = dim(Vδ), which means that to get a suﬃciently small error the
computation time may be extremely large and sometimes even infeasible. This problem is the reason
for using reduced order models (ROM).
The general assumption is that Mδ is actually of low dimension and can therefore be spanned by a
properly chosen low dimensional basis. We call this basis the reduced basis and the associated space
Vrb. Hence, if {ψ}N
n=1 is the reduced basis, then
Vrb = span{ψ1, . . . , ψN} ⊂Vδ,
dim(Vrb) = N,
(4.7)
where N ≪Nδ. When the reduced basis is determined the reduced basis approximation is then given
by:
yrb(µ) =
N
X
i=1
ˆyi
rb(µ)ψi,
(4.8)
where ˆyi
rb(µ) is the i’th generalized Fourier coeﬃcient of the reduced basis approximation [HRS+16].
It is now natural to ask about accuracy.
How well does the RB approximation approximate the
exact solution? Here, we have the following inequality:
||y(µ) −yrb(µ)||V = ||y(µ) −yδ(µ) + yδ(µ) −yrb(µ)||V
≤||y(µ) −yδ(µ)||V + ||yδ(µ) −yrb(µ)||V
≤ϵ + ||yδ(µ) −yrb(µ)||V ,
which shows that the accuracy solely depends on how well yrb(µ) approximates yδ(µ) due to the as-
sumption that the high-ﬁdelity solution can be made arbitrarily precise.
It still remains to be answered how well an N-dimensional subspace can approximate an Nδ-dimensional
manifold. To that extend we introduce the Kolmogorov N-width:
dN(Mδ) = inf
Vrb
sup
yδ∈Mδ
inf
vrb∈Vrb ||yδ −vrb||V .
(4.9)
If the N-width decreases rapidly for increasing dimension of Vrb, then it suggests that the solution
manifold can be well approximated by a low dimensional subspace [HRS+16]. The Kolmogorov N-
width is, in general, a quite diﬃcult measure to be working with and is therefore primarily used for
theoretical purposes.
To assess how well a reduced basis approximates a solution manifold one can consider the problem as
a minimization problem in a least-squares notion:
sZ
µ∈P
inf
vrb∈Vrb ||yδ(µ) −vrb||2 dµ.
(4.10)
4.2.1
Galerkin Projection Method
To arrive at the optimal reduced problem, that is the reduced problem that approximates the high-
ﬁdelity problem optimally, one can consider the procedure as a Galerkin projection. To this end, we
start by deﬁning the inner product, for some bilinear elliptic form a(·, ·;µ),
⟨v, w⟩µ = a(v, w;µ),
(4.11)
==PAGE:44==
30
Reduced Basis Methods for Partial Diﬀerential Equations
and the corresponding energy norm
||v||µ =
q
⟨v, v⟩µ.
(4.12)
Note that this only deﬁnes a norm if a(·, ·;µ) is symmetric for all µ. In the linear case, the high-ﬁdelity
problem is: Given µ ∈P, ﬁnd yδ(µ) ∈Vδ such that
a(yδ(µ), vδ;µ) = f(vδ;µ),
∀vδ ∈Vδ,
(4.13)
and the reduced problem is similarly deﬁned: Given µ ∈P, ﬁnd yrb(µ) ∈Vrb such that
a(urb(µ), vrb;µ) = f(vrb;µ),
∀vrb ∈Vrb.
(4.14)
Considering the error e = yδ(µ) −yrb(µ) in the reduced problem, we get
a(yδ(µ) −yrb(µ), vrb;µ) = a(yδ(µ), vrb;µ) −a(yrb(µ), vrb;µ)
= f(vrb;µ) −f(vrb;µ)
= 0,
which is called the Galerkin orthogonality property, since it shows that the error is orthogonal to the
reduced basis space according to the inner product ⟨·, ·⟩µ [QMN15]. We then get the following from
the orthogonal projection theorem:
Proposition 4.1. Let a(·, ·;µ) be symmetric and coercive. Then the solution yrb(µ) ∈Vrb of (4.14)
satisﬁes
yrb(µ) = arg min

(4.15)
Remark: The coercivity of a(·, ·;µ) is to ensure existence of the solution.

[QMN15]:

Hence, we basically get that given a reduced basis space the optimal projection onto that space is
the orthogonal projection and the error depends on the continuity and coercivity of the bilinear form
a(·, ·;µ).
Even though proposition 4.1 and 4.16 assumes coercivity and linerarity, which is e.g. not the case
for nonlinear PDE-constrained optimization problems, we have still chosen to include the results for
simplicity. This is due to the fact that there are very similar results for these more complicated cases.
Regarding the nonlinearity, we have already seen in section 2.2.3 solving a nonlinear problems boils
down to solving a series of linear ones. Furthermore, the orthogonal projection is still the optimal
projection, but when it comes to well-posedness there are few extra assumptions to be fulﬁlled, similar
to those of the Babuska Theorem, which we will not go into details with here. Instead we refer to
[QMN15].

4.2.3
The Oﬄine and Online Stage
We can enable the aﬃne dependence on the parameters to divide the computations into two distinct
parts [QMN15]:
• The Oﬄine Stage: Assembling the reduced matrices and vectors.
• The Online Stage: Solving the reduced algebraic system.
Oﬄine Stage
This stage is the computationally heaviest. Ideally, all the "heavy lifting" will be done in this stage,
since we only see a direct dependence on Nδ here. At least that is the case for aﬃne problems. We
will see later that this is not the case for non-aﬃne problems.
In this stage the reduced matrices and vectors, Aq
rb and f q
rb, will be assembled and stored.
This
procedure is outlined in Algorithm 2. If by nvv we denote the number of operations required for
a vector-vector product of Nδ-dimensional vectors and nmv the number of operations to compute a
matrix-vector product, then one can show that the required operations in the oﬄine phase is [QMN15]:
O(QfNnvv + Qa(Nnmv + N 2nvv)).
One sees that there is heavy dependence on the high-ﬁdelity dimension.
Algorithm 2 Oﬄine Computations
1: procedure ProjectSystem(Aq
δ, fq
δ, W)
2:
for q = 1 . . . Qa do
3:
Aq
rb = W T Aq
δW
4:
end for
5:
for q = 1 . . . Qf do
6:
fq
rb = W T fq
δ
7:
end for
8:
return Aq
rb, fq
rb
9: end procedure
Online Stage
In this stage we assemble the reduced matrices and vectors and solve the reduced system given the
parameter, µ. As mentioned, these computations are independent from Nδ in the aﬃne case due to
==PAGE:48==
34
Reduced Basis Methods for Partial Diﬀerential Equations
the computations in the oﬄine stage. An outline of the procedure is sketched in Algorithm 3. The
amount of operations needed in the online phase is [QMN15]
O(QaN 2 + QfN).
Thus, there is no dependence on the high-ﬁdelity dimension. Note that this only holds for cases with
completely aﬃne dependence on the parameters.
Algorithm 3 Online Computations
1: procedure SolveRBSystem(Aq
rb, fq
rb, θq
a, θq
f,µ)
2:
Arb(µ) = 0, frb(µ) = 0
3:
for q = 1 . . . Qa do
4:
Arb(µ) = Arb(µ) + θq
a(µ)Aq
rb
5:
end for
6:
for q = 1 . . . Qf do
7:
frb(µ) = frb(µ) + θq
f(µ)fq
rb
8:
end for
9:
Solve Arb(µ)yrb(µ) = frb(µ)
10:
return yrb(µ)
11: end procedure
Workﬂow
The full workﬂow is summarised below:
• Oﬄine Stage:
1. Make high-ﬁdelity discretization of the problem.
2. Generate solutions using high-ﬁdelity discretization.
3. Compute reduced basis.
4. Project system matrices, Aq, and right hand sides, f q, to reduced basis space using (4.25)
and (4.26).
• Online Stage:
1. Assemble reduced system using (4.21).
2. Solve reduced system.
3. Recover solution in high-ﬁdelity space.
We have now described the general ideas and workﬂow of the full process. It is important to notice
that there is no dependence on the high-ﬁdelity problem in the online stage. We simply need to solve
the reduced system and then transform back to the high-ﬁdelity space.
We call these completely
decoupled reduced order models non-intrusive [HU18]. We will see later that this property is far from
always present, but is very important to ensure signiﬁcant speed-ups in most cases.
4.3

In the previous section, we have considered y(µ) = y(x;µ) as the solution to (4.3). We will now consider
the slightly more general time-dependent problem, also called an evolution equation or dynamical

4.4
Nonlinear Problems
Nonlinearities often causes computational diﬃculties and reduced basis methods is unfortunately not
an exception. We will in this subsection go through why nonlinearities cause problems and how to
overcome them. But ﬁrst, we will brieﬂy go over the theory of parametrized nonlinear PDEs
4.4.1
Parametrized Nonlinear PDEs
The general parametrised nonlinear PDE is on the form
G(y(µ);µ) = 0,
G : V × P →V ′,
(4.40)
==PAGE:50==
36
Reduced Basis Methods for Partial Diﬀerential Equations
and it’s corresponding variational (weak form) is:
g(y(µ), v;µ) = ⟨G(y(µ);µ), v⟩= 0,
∀v ∈V.
(4.41)
In the dynamic case, it is often written
∂ty(t;µ) + N(y(t;µ);µ) = 0,
(4.42)
and the weak form
⟨∂ty(t;µ), v⟩+ ⟨N(y(t;µ);µ), v⟩= 0,
(4.43)
accompanied by an initial condition. Here, N : V × P →V ′ is a nonlinear parametrized operator.
By utilizing the θ-scheme, and deﬁning
G(yk
δ (µ);µ) = 1
∆tM
 yk
δ (µ) −yδ(µ)k−1
+ θN k(yk
δ (µ);µ) + (1 −θ)N k−1(yδ(µ)k−1;µ),
(4.44)
the time advancement of the system is on the form of eq. (4.40). Hence, from hereon we will ignore
the superscript, denoting the time step, and simply discuss how to solve eq. (4.40).
Referring to the Fréchet derivative, or equivalently the Gâteaux derivative, in deﬁnition 2.7, we will
consider
DyG(z,µ) : V →V ′,
and
DµG(z,µ) : P →V ′,
as the partial Fréchet derivative of the parametrized nonlinear mapping with respect to y at z and µ
respectively. We then have the following theorem of well-posedness [QMN15]:
Theorem 4.2. Let G : V × P →V ′ be a C1 map and suppose that
1. G(y0;µ0) = 0 for some y0 ∈V and µ0 ∈P.
2. DyG(y0;µ0) : V →V ′ is bijective.
Then there exists r0, r > 0 and a unique y(µ) ∈Br(y0) ∩V such that
G(y(µ);µ) = 0,
µ ∈Br0(µ0) ∩P.
Furthermore, the map µ 7→y(µ) is continuously diﬀerentiable in a suitable open neighborhood of µ0
and
∂y(µ)
∂µ
= −(DyG(y(µ);µ))−1DµG(y(µ);µ).
Note that the Br(x0) denotes an open ball with radius r around x0. A proof of the theorem heavily
relies on the Implicit Function Theorem [Cia13] and is given in [Zei86]. Furthermore, to ensure that
assumption 1 is fulﬁlled one would often make use of theorems like the Banach Fixed Point Theorem
[Zei86], which can often be a diﬃcult task on its own [QMN15].
Basically, theorem 4.2 states that if there is a solution to (4.40) (assumption 1) and the Fréchet
derivative at this solution is bijective (assumption 2), then there exists a local family of solutions to
(4.40) parametrized by the parameter µ around µ0. This insight is incredibly valuable since it ensures
that it makes sense to search for solutions for several parameter values and time instances.
==PAGE:51==
4.4 Nonlinear Problems
37
4.4.2
Reduced Basis Approximation
Once it is established that a solution branch for (4.40) exists, it is then of great interest to know how
to compute such solutions. To this end, we have Newtons method described in section 2.2.3.

Hence, the ﬁnal reduced linear system to be solved in each Newton iteration is

Remark. We have derived the linear system to be solved for the increment in the Newton method.
One could derive, by using the same approach, a system to be solved for the next iterate instead.
In the linear case we were able to completely decouple the oﬄine and the online stage which led
to a so called non-intrusive model. In the nonlinear case we cannot avoid doing computations in the
high-ﬁdelity space, since we have to assemble the Jacobian and residual in the high-ﬁdelity space in
every iteration. This means that the only computations that can be contained in the oﬄine stage is the
construction of the transformation matrix W and potential speed-ups must come from comparatively
large speed-ups in solving a reduced system considering the extra computation time in assembling it.
Whether or not this results in speed-ups at all is highly dependent on the problem.
If one wants to decouple the two stages completely, as for the linear aﬃne case, one would have to use
other methods. A common choice is the empirical interpolation method [QMN15], [HRS+16]. Another
approach is to generate an equation free surrogate model that parametrizes the solution manifold. We
will utilize the latter approach using neural networks.
4.5
Reduced Basis Methods for Optimal Control
As mentioned in section 3.2.5 there are huge computational costs associated with PDE-constrained
optimal control. Especially in the nonlinear cases where repeated solving of large systems is necessary.
Therefore there is a great interest in methods to decrease the size of the resulting saddle-point system.
In this section, we will outline how a reduced basis approach can aid in this matter.
4.6
Parametrized Optimal Control
As for the parametrized PDEs, we now consider both the state and the object functional as being
dependent on not only time and space but also the parameters. Hence, by deﬁning, y(µ) = y(x, t;µ),
u(µ) = u(x, t;µ) and p(µ) = p(x, t;µ) to ease the notation, we restate (3.31):

noted, that this is not necessary, but is simply chosen to simplify the derivations. The dimension
of the reduced space should be chosen based on the complexity of the respective solution manifolds.

When we discussed how to solve the PDE-constrained problems in section 3.2, we introduced the
strategies discretize-then-optimize and optimize-then-discretize. Using a reduced basis approach leads
to two very similar strategies in concept. Namely, reduce-then-optimize and optimize-then-reduce.
When using the strategy reduce-then-optimize one starts by utilizing the reduced versions of the
state and control and thereafter applies an optimization scheme. Hence, one starts by deﬁning the
reduced control problem

From hereon, one would introduce a spatial and temporal discretization and solve the algebraic prob-
lem.
We will, however, focus on the optimize-then-reduce approach.
Here, one starts by deriving the
optimality conditions on the inﬁnite dimensional level and then reduce the problem. In this case, one
will arrive at the nonlinear system in eq. (3.9). To solve this we follow the approach presented in
section 4.4.2 and start with the general nonlinear problem

Hence, in cases where Nδ ≫NT there is potentially much to gain, since it means a large reduction in
the system.
There are a few things worth noting here. Firstly, as mentioned earlier in the discussion of reduced
basis approaches to nonlinear problems, it is not possible to decouple the online stage completely
from the oﬄine. By this we mean that when solving (4.63) in every iteration we need the transform
back to the high-ﬁdelity space to assemble the matrices and the right hand side. Hence, the potential
computational time savings are very limited and sometimes even not present.
Secondly, regarding the constrained problem, one would have to solve the reduced basis problem, then
transform the reduced adjoint solution to the high-ﬁdelity adjoint space and then project the high-
ﬁdelity adjoint state onto the admissible control space. Comparing to the overall computation time
spent on repeatedly solving the system, this is far from a big issue.
Lastly, it is worth noting that we only reduce the spatial dimension and not the temporal.
4.7
Proper Orthogonal Decomposition
Until now we have only discussed how to set up and solve the reduced problems. We still need a very
important component for all the previous discussions to make sense: How to compute the reduced
basis spanning the RB space.
There are several approaches to generating the RB spaces. The two arguably most famous techniques
are the proper orthogonal decomposition (POD) [HRS+16], [QMN15],[Ubb17] and the greedy algorithm
[HRS+16], [QMN15][Haa17]. In this section we will review the POD approach for reduced basis gen-
eration. Combining the POD basis generation with the Galerkin method, described in the previous
sections, leads to scheme we call the POD-Galerkin scheme (POD-G).
==PAGE:56==
42
Reduced Basis Methods for Partial Diﬀerential Equations
POD is an explore-and-compress strategy to generate a basis [HRS+16].
One starts by exploring
the solution manifold by solving the high-ﬁdelity problem for several parameters and then compresses
and only keep the essential information. The method is closely related to principle component analy-
sis (PCA), which is widely used, especially, in data analysis when dealing with high-dimensional data
[QMN15], [FHT01].
In the dicsrete case, the N-dimensional POD-space is deﬁned as the space, Vrb, that minimizes the
P-discrete version of (4.10):

Note that this formulation is similar to what is "normally" used in PCA [FHT01]. Namely, one seeks
a new basis that maximizes the variance of the projected data instead of focusing on choosing a basis
that minimizes the distance between the projected and the original data. The two formulations are,
of course, equivalent.
To actually solve (4.68) numerically we need to choose speciﬁc parameter values, so that we have
solutions yδ(µ) to base our reduced basis upon. To that end, we deﬁne a collection of snapshots:
{yδ(µ1), . . . , yδ(µns)} = {yδ(µ) | µ ∈Θ} ,
(4.69)
where Θ ⊂P. From the collection of snapshots we can deﬁne
MΘ = span {yδ(µ1), . . . , yδ(µns} ⊂RNδ.
(4.70)
Ideally MΘ = Mδ, but it is impossible to explore the full parameter space, nonetheless, MΘ does
act as a good representation when the point-set Θ is chosen with a high enough resolution and with
a good sampling strategy [GH18].

Proposition 4.3 states that the error on the snapshots in the POD basis is equal to the sum of squares
of the singular values corresponding to the neglected POD modes. This means that since the singular
values are decreasing we get the best approximation by using the singular vectors corresponding to
ﬁrst singular values and then add the next in line for higher precision. Furthermore, Proposition 4.3
gives us a way to choose a minimal dimension of the POD space that ensures a desired error. Say that
εP OD is the desired projection error, then one should choose the smallest dimension, N, such that
I(N) =
PN
i=1 σ2
i
Pr
i=1 σ2
i
≥1 −ε2
P OD.
(4.89)
(4.89) is the energy captured by the ﬁrst N modes compared to energy captured by all modes with non-
zero singular values and is called the relative information content of the POD modes. The strategy of
choosing the smallest N such that (4.89) holds translates to choosing N such that the energy retained
by the last r −N modes is smaller than or equal to ε2
P OD [QMN15]. It can be shown that the criterion
ensures that
||S −SN||F
||S||F
≤εP OD,
(4.90)
where SN is the N-rank approximation of S [QMN15].
The full discrete POD procedure is outlined in Algorithm 4.
Algorithm 4 POD Algorithm
1: procedure POD(S, εP OD)
2:
if ns < Nδ then
3:
Solve the eigenvalue problem ST Sζ i = λiζ
4:

1
√λi Sζ
5:
else

7:
end if
8:
Choose the minimal N such that I(N) ≥1 −ε2
P OD holds
9:
return W = [ψ1 | . . . |ψN]
10: end procedure

4.7.2
Parameter Sampling
As mentioned in the beginning of Section 4.7, MΘ ≈Mδ when Θ is chosen ﬁne enough. Unfortunately,
it is, to the best of our knowledge, not possible to ensure a certain precision. This means that the
process is a question of trial and error or simply choosing Θ very ﬁne. To increase the chance of
making a suﬃcient sampling one must ensure that the discrete solution manifold is explored as much
as possible. To ensure that, one must use a proper sampling strategy. Common choices are tensorial
grids, latin hypercube sampling (LHS), or random sampling, etc. [QMN15]. Tensorial sampling might
be ﬁne for low dimensions, but are not suitable for higher dimensions due to the curse of dimensionality.
While random sampling seems like a good choice at ﬁrst, one risks not covering the full parameter
space and thereby leaves parts of the solution manifold unexplored. The LHS solves that problem while
also keeping the uniform distribution of points [QMN15]. In ﬁgure 4.2 we have plotted the realization
of three diﬀerent sampling strategies: (a) the LHS, (b) random and (c) tensorial. We see that the LHS
covers most of the domain while the random sampling tends to leave large areas unexplored. In higher
dimension this problem becomes even more apparent. Hence, we choose to use the LHS method for
sampling parameters. For an in-depth discussion of the sampling strategies, see [Coc07]
4.7.3

Very often, one would not need to compute a reduced basis for the control, since only the adjoint

spanning the control space.
Combining the POD basis generation and the SQP scheme for the optimal control problem gives
a method we will refer to as the POD-SQP method. One could also denote it the POD-Galerkin-SQP
method to emphasize the Galerkin method involved in the process.
==PAGE:65==
Chapter 5
Artiﬁcial Neural Networks
In the previous chapters we saw that solving optimal control problems is a matter of solving a huge
linear (saddle point) system repeatedly, which led to the development of reduced basis methods in
order to decrease the size of these systems. However, for nonlinear control problems it is not possible
to decouple the oﬄine and online phases completely. In order to decouple them completely, one would
have to resort to other methods, e.g. empirical interpolation methods or, as we will do here, regression
models. Our goal is to compute such a regression model using artiﬁcial neural networks.
In this chapter we start by discussing the general theory behind artiﬁcial neural networks and elaborate
on why they are a good choice for our speciﬁc problem.
From thereon, we will explain how one
trains an artiﬁcial neural network and what to be aware of in this process. Then we will establish
the connection between neural networks, optimal control, and reduced basis theory. Lastly, we will
present the programming framework PyTorch.
5.1
The General Theory
Artiﬁcial neural networks are, as the name suggests, inspired by the neuronal networks in the human
brain [Kri07]. However, in this thesis, instead of discussing to what degree artiﬁcial neural networks
resembles that of real neural networks, we will simply consider an artiﬁcial neural network a mathe-
matical/computational model which has a very convenient network representation. Furthermore, from
hereon, we will simply refer to the artiﬁcial neural networks as neural networks or use the abbreviation
NN.
In short, a neural network is a model capable of learning from observational data. It consists of a
series of processing units, also called neurons, which are connected by weighted vertices. Hence, a
neural network can be considered an oriented graph. The formal deﬁnition is given below [Kri07]:
Deﬁnition 5.1 (Artiﬁcial Neural Network). A neural network is a sorted triple (N, V, w), where N is
a set of neurons and V is a set {(i, j) | i, j ∈N} whose elements are called connections between neuron
i and j. The function, w : V →R, is called a weight, where w((i, j)), often abbreviated wi,j, is the
weight of the connection between neuron i and j.
Remark. By convention wi,j = 0 means that neuron i and j are not directly connected.
The above deﬁnition is, as mentioned, a formal one. It does not provide much information about
how one actually utilizes this model. Hence, we will now dive deeper into the structure of neural
networks.
==PAGE:66==
52
Artiﬁcial Neural Networks
5.1.1
The Neurons
A neuron j is connected to m sending neurons, {s1, . . . , sm}, and n receiving neurons, {r1, . . . , rn}.
Denoting by ysk(t) the output "ﬁred", from the neuron sk, the neuron j receives the weighted input
wsk,jysk(t) from each neuron sk, k = 1, . . . m. The parameter t denotes a discrete time variable to
keep track of when neurons are ﬁring and receiving. The neuron j will then be ﬁring yj(t + ∆) at
the next time instance, t + ∆t, to a receiving neuron, ri, which receives wj,riyj(t + ∆t). To ease the
notation we leave out the explicit dependence on the time parameter.
Each neuron in a network is deﬁned by three functions: the propagation function, the activation func-
tion, and the output function [Ubb17]. All of which will be described below.
The Propagation Function.
The propagation function, fprop : R2m →R, takes the vectorial
weighted output, (ys1, . . . , ysm)T , from the previous neurons and the weights, (ws1,j, . . . , wsm,j)T , and
converts it into a scalar:
uj = fprop(ws1,j, . . . , wsm,j, ys1, . . . , ysm).
The typical choice is simply a weighted sum of the outputs,
fprop(ws1,j, . . . , wsm,j, ys1, . . . , ysm) =
m

(5.1)

In many applications, the same activation function are used for all neurons in the network with only
the threshold varying. The threshold often to be decided in a training phase which will be described
later.
The activation function should be chosen based on the problem at hand. There are, of course, many
common choices, some of which, we will present here. Firstly, there is the class called Sigmoid func-
tions. This type of function is s-shaped, strictly increasing, globally diﬀerentiable, and attains values
in a bounded interval [Hay94]. Arguably, the most famous example is the logistic function:

The described neuron is denoted a computation neuron, since it receives an input and computes an

computing anything.
Conclusively, a computation neuron deﬁnes a map that takes input from a series of neurons and

5.1.2
Multilayer Feedforward Neural Networks
Until now, we have only considered a single neuron. The idea of a neural networks is, as the name
suggest, to build a network of these neurons. The way they are connected is what we call the network
topology. There are, in general, two diﬀerent classes of neural networks
1. Feedforward Network. Also called a perceptron. Here, the neurons are arranged in layers.
It consists of a source/input layer with MI source neurons, K hidden layers, each with Hk
computation neurons in each and a single output layer with MO computations neurons. An
important characteristic is the fact that each neuron is only connected to neurons in the next
layer towards the output layer and information can only propagate from the input towards the
output[Ubb17].
2. Recurrent Network. Here, any neuron can be connected to any neuron. Hence, it contains
feedback loops which distinguishes it from the feedforward network.
==PAGE:69==
5.1 The General Theory
55
Figure 5.3: Structure of a feedforward neural network with two inputs, two hidden layers with four
neurons each and a single output neuron [Wol18].
It should be noted that sometimes feedforward networks are divided into to two distinct classes:
multilayer and single-layer. In the single-layer network there are no hidden layers, but only an input
layer (not counted) and an output layer. On the other hand, a multilayer network contains at least one
hidden layer. When the network consists of several hidden layers they are often denoted deep neural
networks. In ﬁgure 5.3 one sees a feedforward neural network with two hidden layers. There are, of
course, much more to be said about each class, but in this thesis we will focus solely on the multilayer
perceptron (feedforward network) due to it’s approximation properties, which will be detailed below.
In general, perceptrons can be considered a map consisting of a series of function compositions. The
input is then propagated through these compositions and in that way computes an output. We often
call the input features and the output responses. E.g. in the case of a single hidden layer with H
neurons, a single feature as input and a single response, a perceptron is simply a function f : R →R
which can be written in the following manner:
f(x) = fout

function and a weighted sum, with bias in the hidden layer and no bias in the output layer (which is

5.1.2.1
Approximation Properties
The relevance of multilayer perceptrons becomes clear when their approximation properties are pre-
sented.
We start by deﬁning the set of functions that can be represented as a single hidden layer perceptron:
Deﬁnition 5.2. The set of all functions that can be represented as a single hidden layer perceptron
with H neurons is deﬁned as:
RH
