The ﬁrst result we will present is the following [Wol18]:
Theorem 5.3 (Approximation of Vectorial Functions). Let d, d′ ∈N, K ⊆Rd be compact and σ : R →
R be either (i) continuous and non-polynomial or (ii) bounded with limx→−∞σ(x) ̸= limx→∞σ(x).
Then Rd,d′(σ) is dense in the space of continuous functions f : K →Rd′.
Remark: Dense in this case could, for example, be in the following norm:
||f|| :=

{||fi||∞}d′
i=1


′
,
(5.10)
where ||·||′ is any norm on Rd′ [Wol18].
Hence, by theorem 5.3 we can approximate any vectorial continuous function with a single hidden
layer perceptron. While this is a very strong result it is of further interest to uncover if the same goes
for deep networks. To this end we start by the following lemma [Wol18]:
Lemma 5.4 (Conversion from Shallow to Deep Networks). Consider the class of layered feed-forward
neural networks with MI input and MO output nodes and activation functions that are taken from a
ﬁxed set that includes the identity. Within this class, every function representable with a single hidden
layer of H neurons admits a representation by a network with H hidden layers, each of which contains
at most MI + MO + 1 neurons.
With this in mind the following result is easily obtained.
Theorem 5.5 (Approximation by Deep Networks). Let d, d′ ∈N, K ⊆Rd be compact and σ : R →R
be either (i) continuous and non-polynomial or (ii) bounded with limx→−∞σ(x) ̸= limx→∞σ(x). Then
any continuous function, f : K →Rd′, can be approximated arbitrarily well by a deep feed-forward
neural network with at most d + d′ + 1 neurons in ||·||∞.
Proof. By ﬁrst using theorem 5.3 to conclude that there exists a single hidden layer feed-forward
network that approximates f and then using lemma 5.4 to convert this shallow network to a deep one,
one has proved the theorem.
==PAGE:71==
5.2 Training a Neural Network
57
A consequence of Theorem 5.5 is that that any continuous vectorial function can be represented by a
deep neural network. This result is incredibly important, since it backs up numerous modern applica-
tions of deep neural networks. We will refer to this collection of results as the universal approximation
properties.
The above discussed results are what ensures the foundation for using neural networks in function
approximation problems. While these are very important results, they do not answer any practical
questions such as:
• How many neurons are necessary to ensure a certain accuracy?
• Does it make sense to use deeper networks? If so, when?
• When is one activation function more suitable?
• How do we compute the neural networks, i.e. the weigths?
The last question will be tackled in the next section. Regarding the ﬁrst two, there is much active
work being done at the moment. In [MP16] it is shown that the approximation properties of a neural
network is dependent on the regularity of the function under consideration.
That is, the higher
regularity, the fewer neurons necessary. Furthermore, in [MP16], [ABMM16] and [HLXZ18] the need
for deep networks are discussed. It is argued that even though single hidden layer perceptrons are
suﬃcient it is sometimes much more eﬃcient to use two or more. This is due to the fact that using a
single hidden layer often means that a huge amount of neurons are necessary where when using more
layers one can often use much fewer neurons per layer.
This is shown in ﬁgure 5.4, where we have tried to approximate a cylindrical shaped height function
by two neural network conﬁgurations1. One with a single hidden layer with 100 neurons, see ﬁgure
5.4a, and one with two hidden layers with only 3 neurons in each layer, see ﬁgure 5.4b. Using (5.9) to
compute the number of weights to compute in each case, we see that the single hidden layer network
has 302 weights, while the two hidden layer network only has 25. Even though there is signiﬁcantly
more weights to compute in the one hidden layer case, the two hidden layer network performs much
better. This is qualitatively clear just by looking at the two ﬁgures, but also quantitatively by looking
at the relative mean squared error which is 0.0015 in the two layer case and 0.2449 in the one layer
case.
This small example is a clear motivator for looking into deep neural networks even though the theory
says that a single layer should be enough.
5.2
Training a Neural Network
In the previous section we have presented the structure and elements of feedforward neural networks.
Furthermore, the reasoning behind the great interest in these function approximators has been pre-
sented in shape of the universal approximation theorems showcasing the approximation properties of
neural networks. An essential aspect of dealing with neural networks is yet to be discussed, namely
how to compute the weights. This is the exact problem we deal with in this section.
Computing the weights is what we call training the network or the learning process. The training
procedure is done by exposing the network to some training patterns, also called a training set. The
weights are then adjusted according to some rules. After the training the weights should be computed
such that new features, even features the network has not seen before, will result in responses that
seem reasonable. This property is known as generalization [Kri07].
1For this small setup we have used Matlab code provided in the DTU course 02582, Computational Data Analysis,
in the spring 2018.
==PAGE:72==
58
Artiﬁcial Neural Networks
(a) One hidden layer with 100 neurons
(b) Two hidden layers with 3 neurons in each
layer.
Figure 5.4:
The grey surface is the functions to be approximated and the red grid is the neural
network approximation. The Network with two hidden layers, but much fewer neurons, outperforms
the single hidden layer network with many neurons.
Based on the nature of the training set there are diﬀerent learning paradigms. Overall there are three
diﬀerent paradigms [Ubb17]:
Supervised Learning. Here the training set consists of both features and corresponding responses
also called labeled data. The input features is a set of vectors, {xi}Ntr
i=1, and the corresponding responses
is likewise a set of vectors, {yi}Ntr
i=1. The training procedure then aims to minimize the distance be-
tween the responses, yi, and the responses computed by the network, ˜yi = F(xi), for some features,
xi. The goal is thereby often to make predictions for future data based on previous data [FHT01].
Unsupervised Learning. In contrast to supervised learning the training patterns in unsupervised
learning is unlabeled. The training set only consists of features and the task of the network is then to
group the data. In these cases the goal is often to discover (hidden) patterns in data [FHT01].
Reinforcement Learning. In this paradigm the network is given some features and based on the
computed responses the network is given some value, also called feedback or reward. The learning
process should then encourage weight computation that results in the largest rewards.
For our problem the supervised learning paradigm is deﬁnitely the most suitable. Hence, we will
not go into further details with the other two.
From hereon, we will consider a neural network a map
F : RMI →RMO,
x 7→˜y = F(x),
(5.11)
that maps features to responses. The aim of the learning procedure is then to compute the weights
such that F approximates some unknown function f : RMI →RMO. Hence, the aim is to minimize
||F −f|| over some domain V ⊂RMI, where ||·|| is some suitable norm on RMO. The training data
consists of inputs and outputs of the unknown function,
P = {pi}Ntr
i=1 = {(xi, yi) = (xi, f(xi))}Ntr
i=1.
A successful training results in accurate responses for the training data, that is
F(xi) ≈yi,
(xi, yi) ∈P.
This means that the training of a network is a regression/data ﬁtting problem. We formulate this by
deﬁning a loss function over the training set:
L : RNw →R,
w 7→L(w),
==PAGE:73==
5.2 Training a Neural Network
59
where Nw is the total number of weights. Note that we consider w as the vector consisting of all the
weights as well as biases. Then the solution to the training/regression problem is
w∗= argminw∈RNw L(w).
The choice of loss function is dependent on the problem at hand. For classiﬁcation problems the cross-
entropy is a common choice [FHT01]. Often the mean squared error (MSE) is chosen for regression
problems, such as the one we are facing. Here, we start by deﬁning the speciﬁc error, being the error
for each training input, p, as the squared l2  RMO

as the mean of all the speciﬁc errors:
L(w) = MSE(w)

Minimizing L is a very complicated and time consuming aﬀair. This is especially due to the following
problems:
• L is nonlinear.
• L is not convex.
• The parameter space is of very high dimension.
The nonlinearity means that we need to use iterative algorithms. That is, we update wk according to
some rule
wk = wk−1 + δwk−1.
The second problem means that we must be aware of the problem of ending up in local minima instead
of the global minimum. We will address this problem along the way following a discussion on how
to choose an appropriate minimization algorithm. The last problem gives very large computation
time if one is not choosing a suitable algorithm. There are many types of methods that converge fast
but depends on the dimension, e.g. Newton type methods where one has to solve a linear system in
every iteration. Solving such a system becomes ineﬃcient or even infeasible for very large dimensions.
Furthermore, higher order methods are only local solvers. This means that their performance depends
on a good initial guess. In high-dimensional non-convex problems, this is really diﬃcult to provide.
On the other hand we have gradient methods which are essentially independent of the dimension in
the sense that the number of functions approximations are constant up to some accuracy and they
are ensured to converge to a (local) minimum [Wol18]. Thus, we will continue working with gradient
methods. Which means
δwk−1 = αk ∂L(wk−1)
∂w
.
(5.13)
==PAGE:74==
60
Artiﬁcial Neural Networks
αk is traditionally called the step length in the case of line search algorithms [NW06]. However, within
machine learning communities it is often denoted the learning rate [FHT01], as will we for the rest of
the thesis. As an example, we have the steepest decent method. Here αk is simply some negative scalar,
which results in a step along the steepest decent direction. While this a simple and easy to understand
method, there are much better and advanced choices, some of which, we will present below. Common
for all the algorithms, we will present, is that it is necessary to compute the gradient ∂L
∂w. Due to the
nonlinearities and large dimensions, this is not a trivial task to do in a way that is both eﬃcient and
accurate.
5.2.1
Backpropagation
A common method for computing the gradient is known as Backpropagation. To many people, not
working with deep learning, this is a special case of what is also known as automatic diﬀerentiation.
It diﬀers from numerical diﬀerentiation, such as ﬁnite diﬀerence, since it does not use an approxima-
tion scheme to compute derivatives. Furthermore, it diﬀers from symbolic diﬀerentiation since every
building block of the function is computed immediately and is not kept on a symbolic level. This
makes up a very eﬃcient method for computing derivatives by utilizing the structure of the network
by careful bookkeeping [Wol18].
Consider a layered feedforward network with layers l ∈{0, . . . , m}.
l = 0 is the input layer and
l = m is the output layer. We let Hl denote the number of neurons in layer l. By wl

connecting the two layers in a matrix, wl. Similarly we denote the bias θl
j oﬀneuron j in layer l. We
denote the output of a layer, l, by yl ∈RHl. The components of that vector, yl

of each neuron, j, in the layer l. We will only consider the case of identity output functions. Hence,

l −1 before applying the activation function.

We thereby see that we get the derivative of the layers by propagating the error back from the output
layer. Hence, the name backpropagation. With this formulation it is possible to only do one forward
propagation, where one computes ul and yl for all l, and one backpropagation, where one computes
δl for all l. This is possible if one makes sure to carefully save all the information when propagating
forward and backward respectively. This is a much more eﬀective way of computing the derivative
than traditional numerical diﬀerentiation by e.g. ﬁnite diﬀerences, where one has to "start over" for
each weight instead of reusing information.
The drawback of using backpropagation is the extra bookkeeping necessary. This, however, is often
eﬀectively handled in modern software frameworks, such as PyTorch [PGC+17].
The variable δl
j includes the derivative of the activation function. Therefore, the choice of activa-
tion function plays a huge role in the training phase. E.g. if one chooses a sigmoidal function, the
gradient will vanish for very large and very small inputs. This will result in incredibly small steps
for the optimizer and slow down training signiﬁcantly. On the other hand, if one chooses the a ReLU
function this problem is avoided since the the gradient is constant.
5.2.2
Stochastic gradient Descent
Once the gradient is computed it is used to update the weights and biases according to some rule.
The arguably simplest approach to this is the steepest decent algorithm with constant learning rate,
α > 0:
δwk−1 = −α∂L(wk−1)
∂w
.
(5.15)
==PAGE:76==
62
Artiﬁcial Neural Networks
Note that wk refers to the k’th iterate of the optimization scheme and must not be confused with the
previous notation for the matrix of weights connecting layers. If we choose the learning rate small
enough and the gradient is L-Lipschitz, the steepest descent algorithm has some pleasant properties
such as [Wol18]
• L(wk) < L(wk−1) unless ∂L
∂w = 0.
• If L is bounded from below then ∂L(wk)
∂w
→0 for k →∞.
Hence, we have ensured monotone convergence towards a stationary point. However, taking a step
in the steepest decent direction is not necessarily the best direction. Furthermore, computing the
gradient using the full training data set is quite expensive when the data set is large and there is, in
general, a lot of redundant information.
To overcome these problems a great and popular solution is to consider a stochastic variant called
stochastic gradient descent (SGD). In this variant we replace the gradient with an estimator of the
gradient, gk(w), which gives an update rule
δwk−1 = −αgk(wk−1).
(5.16)
We will denote gk(wk−1) = gk. The analysis of the SGD method will be kept quite informal and we
will rather give an intuitive understanding of how and why it works so well.
To ensure convergence we need the following to be fulﬁlled:
1. gk should be unbiased. That is,
E[gk] = ∂L(wk−1)
∂w
.
(5.17)
2. The sequence g1, . . . , gK must be i.i.d.
3. V(gk) ≤σ2, for all k = 1, . . . , K.
If this is the case, then for L(w∗) the minimum, we have [Wol18]
E[L(wk) −L(w∗)] ≤(1 −C1α)k(L(w0) −L(w∗)) + C2ασ2.
(5.18)
The constants C1 and C2 are positive constants that depend on the smoothness and convexity of the
function L and does, therefore, not depend on the SGD algortithm. (5.18) states that for convex
functions the SGD converges in mean to a neighborhood of the minimum. Where the size of the
neighborhood is determined by the variance of the estimator. Unlike the steepest descent method we
can therefore not ensure convergence due to the stochastic nature of the method. Even though this
seems like a big problem at ﬁrst glance, one can easily overcome it by choosing a good estimator and
a small enough learning rate. Furthermore, since the method only converges in mean there is no strict
descent as there is for the steepest descent.
A very common choice for the estimator is to compute the stochastic gradient using only a single
randomly chosen training example. That is,
gk = ∂Lp(wk−1)
∂w
.
(5.19)
Often times the strategy of using a single data point to compute the gradient is referred to as the
stochastic gradient descent. Thus, we will refer to this speciﬁc estimator when referring to the SGD.
This way of computing g is unbiased, and if the data is i.i.d. then the gradients in each step are too.
Furthermore, if the gradient of L is bounded then the gradient of Lp is too for all p.
==PAGE:77==
5.2 Training a Neural Network
63
Figure 5.5: Descent paths for BGD, SGD and MBGD. Visualizes the the stochastic behavior of the
descent paths when computing gradient on varying number of data samples. Source: [gra].
This way of, computing the gradient, is extremely cheap. In each iteration a new training sample
is chosen for the gradient computation without replacement. in that way we ensure that all data is
included in the training. A full cycle through the data is normally called an epoch. Note that one
epoch is much more expensive when using the SGD than the traditional steepest descent. However, an
epoch, using SGD, means that one would have taken Ntr steps and not just a single one. In general,
one needs more iterations using SGD but fewer epochs.
Since the descent path is not strict the choice of learning rate is quite important. To ensure that
the neighborhood around the minimum is small enough, one would often have to lower the learning
rate towards the end of the training.
While the steepest descent and SGD are examples of two extremes when it comes to computing
the gradient, there are methods in between. Speciﬁcally, the mini-batch gradient descent (MBGD).
Here one neither uses all the data nor only a single sample, but rather a sub sample of size Nmb, where
Ntr > Nmb > 1. Hence,
gk = ∂LPi(wk−1)
∂wk−1
,
Pi ⊂P.
(5.20)
With the batch terminology, one often calls the steepest descent method batch gradient descent (BGD).
Using the MBGD one lowers the variance part of (5.18) while still keeping the computational cost at
a low, if the mini-batches are small enough. Furthermore, the descent path is more direct than for the
SGD since more information is used to compute the gradient in each iteration. This is visualized in
Figure 5.5, where one see the obvious diﬀerences in descent trails as expected.
As for the SGD one picks a mini-batch, without replacement, in each iteration, then computes the
gradient, take a step and start over until an epoch is completed. One then starts over with the next
epoch. There are no exact rules for choosing the size of the mini-batches. Often times, the size is
considered a hyperparameter to be tuned (see section 5.3 for more details), other times the size is
chosen based on computational eﬃciency. It is, however, important to reshuﬄe the data in each epoch
to ensure that the gradients are i.i.d..
5.2.2.1
Adaptive Moment Estimation
As mentioned earlier, the learning rate is a quite important parameter to be tuned. Quite often it
is not enough to have a single learning rate for all iterations of the gradient descent but rather an
adaptive learning rate. This is especially important for the SGD methods, where a decrease in the
==PAGE:78==
64
Artiﬁcial Neural Networks
Figure 5.6: Gradient descent with and without momentum [cav].
learning rate is essential for getting really close to the actual minimum. It is even advantageous to
have individual learning rates for each parameter in the parameter space.
In recent years there has been done a lot of work on this subject and many diﬀerent variations of
the SGD has been developed, such as SGD with momentum, RMSprop, Adagrad, etc. [ove]. In this
thesis we have chosen to focus on the so called Adaptive Moment Estimation (Adam) algorithm [KB14].
Using gradient descent ensures convergence for small learning rates. However, for loss functions a
gradient dscent step is rarely the optimal direction to update the parameters. In Figure 5.6 one sees
a two-dimensional case where the contours of the loss function are ellipses. The direction is highly
oscillatory and it will take a long time to converge. Hence, we introduce the concept of exponential
moving averages. We deﬁne the momentum to be
mk = β1mk−1 + (1 −β1)gk,
(5.21)
with m0 = 0. This gives an exponential averaging of the (stochastic) gradient, since
mk = β1mk−1 + (1 −β1)gk
= β1
 β1mk−2 + (1 −β1)gk−1
+ (1 −β1)gk
= (1 −β1)
k
X

βi
1gk−i.
The update rule is then
wk = wk−1 −αmk.
(5.22)
As this corresponds to averaging over past gradients we get a much less oscillatory descent path. See
ﬁgure 5.6. This method is often refered to as the gradient descent with momentum.
A second improvement of the gradient descent is to adapt the learning rate for each parameter.
This can be done by introducing the second order momentum:
vk = β2vk−1 + (1 −β2)(gk−1)2

The addition of ϵ in the denominator is simply to avoid division by zero. This modiﬁcation ensures
that the directions with large steps are dampened. This update rule constitutes the scheme called
Root Mean Square Propagation (RMSProp).
The Adam scheme is a combination of the momentum and the RMSProp.
To deﬁne the Adam
update rule we start by noting that since both the ﬁrst and the second moment are initialized as zero
vectors, they have a bias towards zero. This can be corrected in the following way [KB14]:

Very common choices are β1 = 0.9 and β2 = 0.999 [KB14].
The combination of the RMSProp and the momentum should ensure that the descent path does
not suﬀer from spurious oscillations and that the learning rate will adapt during the optimization.
Furthermore, they do not add signiﬁcantly extra computational eﬀort. The method works both for the
batch, mini-batch and stochastic gradient descent and is summarized in Algorithm 5 heavily inspired
by [KB14]. Note that we have not speciﬁed a stopping criteria. Deﬁning a proper stopping criteria is
not a trivial task for training of neural networks. An obvious choice is to stop when the loss is below a
certain tolerance. This criteria is often used and combined with other criteria such as early stopping,
which will be presented in the next section.

5.3
Improving Neural Networks
In the previous sections, we have discussed the structure and the potential of neural networks as ap-
proximators. Furthermore, we have presented optimizers for training. Even though that, in principle,
should be enough to ensure precision, there is still a lot of issues to take care of. Especially how to
deal with the non-convexity of the loss function and the question of generalization.
==PAGE:80==
66
Artiﬁcial Neural Networks
Bias-Variance Trade-oﬀ
When speaking of generalization one is referring to the question of how well the model will perform
for unseen data. To this end we introduce the expected prediction error (EPE) for an input feature
x0:
EPE(x) = E
h
||y0 −F(x0)||2i
=
σ2
|{z}
irreducible error
+ bias2(F(x0))
|
{z
}
bias
+ V [F(x0)]
|
{z
}
variance
.
Thus, the EPE is composed of three parts: i) an irreducible error, ii) a bias and iii) a variance. Often
when either the bias or the variance is minimized the other one will increase. One therefore has to
balance the two [FHT01].
Regularization
Having a very small bias but large variance is typically a symptom of overﬁtting to training data. If
the model ﬁts the training data too well, it might not be able to generalize properly. One way to
decrease the variance, at cost of increased bias, is to include a regularization term in the loss function
[FHT01]. A common choice is l2 regularization on the weights:
L(w) = MSE(w) + λ ||w||2
l2 .
This way, large values of the weights get penalized and it forces the network to not rely on individual
neurons too much. If one chooses λ = 0 one recovers the original loss function. Furthermore, if one
chooses λ too big, the model might not be able learn from the data properly.
Another common technique to prevent overﬁtting is early stopping.
This method is quite simple.
The idea is to stop the optimization algorithm before it adapts to the training data too well. Here,
one would have a validation set and measure the error on this while training. When the validation
error starts to increase one would stop the training and use the weights with the lowest validation
error.
Hyperparameter Tuning
In models such as neural networks we have hyperparameters. These are parameters that deﬁne the
model but is not adjusted in the training phase. Hence, the weights and biases would not be considered
hyperparameters. On the other hand, the learning rate, the size of the mini batches, the number of
layers and neurons, the regularization parameter, etc. would be considered hyperparameters. All of
these inﬂuence the model directly or the training of the model and must therefore be tuned in order
to get the best possible model.
A common procedure for this is cross-validation. Here, one divides the training set into K parts.
Then the training is performed on K −1 of the subsets and the error will then be measured on the
remaining set. This is repeated until every set has been used for validation for all the hyperparameter
values one wanted to test. This way, one ensures that the value of the hyper parameter encourages
generalization of the model.
==PAGE:81==
5.4 Application of Neural Networks to Reduced Order Models
67
Weight Initialization
One of the big problems in training a network is the fact that the loss function in non-convex. There-
fore, the choice of initial guess for the weights is very important in order to avoid local minima and/or
saddle points. A good choice for initializing the weights is a sampling from a centered Gaussian dis-
tribution with variance 1 [Ubb17]. Furthermore, one would have to initialize and train several times
and then pick the model with the lowest error to ensure the best possible result on a given training
set.
5.4
Application of Neural Networks to Reduced Order Models
With all the time spent on how neural networks work and how to train them, it has still not been made
clear how exactly they ﬁt into the framework of reduced order models. The reason for our interest in
neural networks is primarily the approximation properties discussed in section 5.1.2.1.
We saw earlier that for nonlinear PDE problems, including control problems, using the POD approach
for constructing a reduced order model resulted in a scheme still depending on the high-ﬁdelity order.
I.e. the scheme was intrusive. This is potentially a problem if one needs to solve the problem at
hand in real-time. Therefore, we aim to use neural networks to construct a surrogate model which is
equation free. The main idea is the following:
Use neural networks to interpolate the discrete solution manifold parametrized by the
input parameters.
Since neural networks are much faster to evaluate than solving multiple linear systems, a speed-up is
almost guaranteed. However, this comes at a cost of accuracy. This approach is heavily inspired by
[Ubb17] and [HU18]. We call this method the proper orthogonal decomposition - sequential quadratic
programming - neural network (POD-SQP-NN) method.
For this purpose we consider a neural network a map, F, from the parameter space to the POD
basis coeﬃcients of the high-ﬁdelity solution for all times instances:
F : P ⊂RP →RNNT ,
µ 7→F(µ).
(5.25)
The response, F(µ), is a large vector with the reduced basis coeﬃcients for each time instance:
F(µ) = ¯ynn(µ) = (ynn(t1;µ)T , . . . , ynn(tNT ;µ)T )T
(5.26)
where ynn(tk;µ) is a column vector consisting of all the NN approximated reduced basis coeﬃcients
for time t = tk . Once the coeﬃcients are computed, they are transformed to the high-ﬁdelity basis
by multiplying by the transformation matrix. Hence, we aim to approximate the map
µ 7→WT ¯yδ(µ),
(5.27)
where
W =


W
W
...
W

,
and ¯yδ(µ) is the vector consisting of the high-ﬁdelity degrees of freedom for all discrete time instances,
as deﬁned in (3.28). It is not, strictly speaking, necessary to make use of a reduced basis. One could
simply use the neural network to map the parameters to the high-ﬁdelity basis coeﬃcients. This would,
==PAGE:82==
68
Artiﬁcial Neural Networks
however, require a very large network with a large output space, which is not necessarily feasible to
train. With the POD basis functions we can actually achieve the, more or less, same accuracy but
with much fewer parameters to be estimated.
Furthermore, by using a problem dependent set of
bases we have included the physics in the neural networks which increases the chances of a successful
approximation signiﬁcantly.
Due to the diﬀerent nature of the state, control, and the adjoint state we need to train a network
for each of them. Often times one is not interested in the adjoint state, but simply the state and
the control. Hence, it is not necessary to train a network to learn the adjoint state. In the case of
control constrained problems it is, however, necessary to train a network to learn the adjoint state
and thereafter project onto the admissible control space to ensure that the constraints are satisﬁed.
For the training of the network, we generate the training set by sampling parameters, using the
latin hypercube sampling method, and compute high-ﬁdelity solutions and transform them to the
POD basis:
P =

(µi, WT
y ¯yδ(µi))
	Ntr
i=1 .
(5.28)
That is, the features are the parameters and the POD representation of the high-ﬁdelity solutions are
the responses. The transformation matrix and the high-ﬁdelity solution depends on whether one is
dealing with the state, control, or adjoint state.
Since there are, more or less, endless possibilities for the topology of the network we choose to limit
ourselves to only look at perceptrons with three hidden layers and H neurons in each one. This reduces
the search for the optimal network to only look for the number of neurons there should be in each
layer and simpliﬁes the trial-and-error analysis quite a bit. Furthermore, it means that our network
will have the following number of weights to be trained:
Nw(P, N, Nt, H) = (P + 1)H + 2(H + 1)H + (H + 1)NNT .
(5.29)
For example for 3 parameters, 10 POD basis functions, 50 time steps and 10 neurons in each hidden
layer, we have Nw(3, 10, 50, 10) = 5760 weights. Increasing the number of input parameters does not
change the total number of neurons signiﬁcantly. However, changing the number of neurons has a
huge eﬀect.
One of the big problems in machine learning is the lack of data. Since we are generating the data
ourselves, we can minimize this problem as much as we want, assuming no time constraints. This
means after a completed training of a network we can evaluate the error and decide if we are satisﬁed.
If not, assuming the training was done properly using the methods described earlier in this chapter, we
can potentially improve the network by simply generating more training data and continue or restart
the training. The full oﬄine training is summarized in Algorithm 6 which is inspired by [Ubb17].
In line 16 one utilizes the methods to ensure optimal training discussed earlier in the chapter, e.g.
multiple initializaation or using a validation set for early stopping. Futhermore, if one wants to tune
the hyperparameters by e.g. cross validation, this should be included in the function call as well or as
a pre-training procedure.
The overall procedure can be summarized in the following:
1. Oﬄine:
(a) Sample parameters/features using latin hypercube sampling.
(b) Compute snapshot matrix, S, using a high-ﬁdelity method (SQP method).
(c) Compute transformation matrix, W, using Algorithm 4.
(d) Compute an optimal neural network using Algorithm 6.
2. Online

5.4.1
A Priori Error Analysis
It is important to point out that at this point one is dealing with many levels of approximation.
Firstly, one is approximating the exact problem by a high-ﬁdelity discrete problem. This problem is
then approximately solved using a Newton/SQP method. Then it is transformed to a reduced basis
space and lastly these solutions are approximated by a neural network. It is therefore very important
to ensure that each step is precise enough according to some tolerance.
==PAGE:84==
70
Artiﬁcial Neural Networks
We ﬁrst want to motivate the choice of the MSE for the loss function in the neural network training.
To this end, we deﬁne F(µ) = ¯ynn(µ) ∈RNNT to be the response computed by the neural network.
From hereon we will omit the explicit dependence on µ unless it is crucial for the point to be made.
Thus ¯ynn(µ) = ¯ynn. Then taking a closer look at the MSE [Ubb17]:
MSE(¯ynn, WT ¯yδ) ∝
¯ynn −WT ¯yδ

projected high-ﬁdelity solution and the neural network output transformed to the high-ﬁdelity space.

The ﬁrst term, εδ, we call the high-ﬁdelity error. This is the error between the exact solution and
the high-ﬁdelity approximation. This is solely dependent on the chosen scheme and can be bounded
according to the choice:
εδ ≤Eδ
(5.32)
The second term in (5.31), εrb, we call the reduced basis projection error. It is the error between
the high-ﬁdelity solution solution and the projected high-ﬁdelity solution onto the reduced space. By
(4.93) we see that this depends on the l2 error, which, by Proposition 4.3, is the total energy retained
in the singular values belonging to the neglected POD modes. Assuming exponential decay (which is
a fair assumption as we shall see for the test cases) we have
εrb ≈crbe−aN,
(5.33)
for crb, a > 0.
The last term in (5.31), εnn, is the neural network error.
It is the error between the neural net-
work solution in the high-ﬁdelity basis and the projected high-ﬁdelity solution onto the reduced space.
As for the temporal POD error discussion, we assume that the error is on average the same for each
time step, which motivates only looking at the error in space for an arbitrarily chosen time instance.

Note that Enn coincides with the MSE in the training of the network. In practice cnn will be highly
dependent on the number of time steps chosen due to potential training diﬃculties: More time steps
⇒more output neurons ⇒more weights to train. Therefore, one is interested in using as few time
steps as possible. However, using too few results in increasing high-ﬁdelity error. Which emphasizes
that choosing a good high-ﬁdelity time discretization scheme might be crucial.
Furthermore, it is worth noting that increasing the number of POD modes increases the error bound
by a factor
√
N. Thus, it gets increasingly harder to attain a certain accuracy when the amount of
POD modes, i.e. the number of output neurons in the neural network, increases.
Combining the three terms, gives an error bound:
y −yδ
nn

L2(Q) ≤Eδ + crbe−aN + cnn
√
NEnn.
(5.35)
Therefore, the error committed by using the POD-SQP-NN method depends on (i) how good the high-
ﬁdelity method is, (ii) the quality and number of reduced basis functions, and (iii) the approximation
properties of the trained neuralt network. Due to many years of research, we have a solid understanding
and experience in how to lower Eδ in many cases. Regarding the reduced basis projection error, we
have suﬃcient theory to ensure solid bounds in the linear cases. This is unfortunately not the case
for nonlinear problems [Ubb17], [HRS+16], [QMN15]. However, as we shall see for the test cases, the
POD method often provide a substantial decrease in dimension. Lastly, the arguably least understood
and most diﬃcult to drive down, is Enn. There is, unfortunately not a solid theoretical framework
to ensure a certain level of precision. Experience does, however show, that this can often be lowered
when one uses the methods presented earlier in the chapter among other approaches.

72
Artiﬁcial Neural Networks
==PAGE:87==
Chapter 6
Test Case: Time Dependent PDEs
We have in the previous chapters discussed how to solve time dependent PDEs using reduced basis
methods. In this chapter we will present some results regarding convergence, computation time and
other relevant measures of performance. The primary goal of this chapter is to show the performance
of the POD method and not the neural networks. That is, we aim to showcase when a reduced basis
approach is a good strategy.
Firstly, we analyze the 2D linear heat equation with a source term. This is a great example to show-
case how varying some parameters signiﬁcantly lowers the eﬀectiveness of a reduced basis approach.
Secondly, we look at a linear advection-diﬀusion-reaction equation with Robin boundary condition.
The aim is to show how parametrized boundary conditions can be handled. Lastly, we move on to
the nonlinear viscous Burgers’ equation to show how well a small set of basis functions can capture
complicated nonlinear dynamics.
6.1
Linear Heat Equation with Source Term
The problem at hand is given by
∂ty(µ) −κ∆y(µ) = f(µ),
in
Q,
(6.1a)
y(x, t;µ) = 0,
on
Σ,
(6.1b)
y(x, 0;µ) = y0(µ),
in
Ω,
(6.1c)
where Q = Ω× [0, T]. The corresponding weak form is given by
⟨∂ty(t;µ), ϕ⟩+ κ ⟨∇y(t;µ), ∇ϕ⟩= ⟨f(t;µ), ϕ⟩,
∀ϕ ∈V,
(6.2a)
⟨y(0;µ), ϕ⟩= ⟨y0(µ), ϕ⟩,
∀ϕ ∈V.
(6.2b)
This PDE models diﬀusion of heat with an initial heat proﬁle y0 and a heat source f. κ is the diﬀusion
parameter which describes how fast the heat diﬀuses. We consider only the case with homogeneous
boundary conditions.
For this problem, we choose the space V = W(0, T) as the solution space. (6.1) is a special case of
(4.33), with
a(u(µ), v,µ) = κ
Z
Ω
∇u(µ) · ∇v dΩ.
Note that in this case, the aﬃne decomposition is clearly given from the start.
==PAGE:88==
74
Test Case: Time Dependent PDEs
By following the procedure outlined in section 4.3 we arrive at the following discrete system to solve
in order to advance in time using the implicit Euler scheme:
 1
∆tMδ + κKδ

uk
δ(µ) = 1
∆tMδuk−1
δ
(µ) + fk
δ(µ),
(6.3)
where Kδ is the stiﬀness matrix.
The reduced basis approximation is carried out according to Section 4.3 with the POD-basis computed
via Algorithm 4.
For the numerical tets we choose the two-dimensional case with Ω= [−2, 2]×[−2, 2] and time interval
[0, T]. The initial condition is y0 = e−a(x2
1+x2
2), where (x1, x2) ∈Ωand a > 0. This corresponds to an
initial Gaussian heat proﬁle with a determining the width and (0, 0) is the center. The initial condition
for various choices of a is shown in Figure 6.1. Furthermore, we consider heat sources on the form
f(x, t; γ, β) = βe−10((x1−γ)2+(x2−1)2,
γ ∈[−1, 1], β ≥1
This models a Gaussian distributed heat source of magnitude β and center at (γ, 1). It is worth noting
that the right hand side does not depend aﬃnely on the parameter γ. The source term for various
parameter values can be seen in Figure 6.2. With the initial condition, source term, and diﬀusivity
constant the parameter space, P is four dimensional and µ = (κ, a, γ, β).
The parameter space is sampled via the latin hypercube sampling procedure.
For the solution of the high-ﬁdelity problem we used FEniCS for the spatial discretizations. In par-
ticular, we used ﬁrst order ﬁnite elements with 128 degrees of freedom.
To test the eﬀectiveness of the POD approximation we tested two diﬀerent cases:
• Case 1: κ ∈[1, 10], a ∈[5, 10], γ ∈[−1, 1], β ∈[1, 5], 300 snapshots
• Case 2: κ ∈[1, 10], a = 5, γ = 0.7, β ∈[1, 5], 300 snapshots
It is interesting to notice the diﬀerence between the two cases. In case 1 we vary the location of the
heat source as well as the distribution of the initial heat together with the source intensity and the
diﬀusion parameter. In case 2 we only vary the diﬀusion parameter and the source intensity. We
expect the second case to be the most successful due to the much simpler parameter space.
In Figure 6.3a we see the convergence of the two POD methods. Both exhibit convergence some-
where between spectral and polynomial. However, for case 2 the convergence is shown to be better, as
expected. This means that more POD functions are needed to capture the varying dynamics of a heat
source with varying placement. In Figure 6.3b the eigenvalues are plotted for both cases. Here, we
see a large diﬀerence. With the constant location of the source term the eigenvalues decreases much
faster than for the varying location case. Which further supports the hypothesis that there is a need
for more basis functions to approximate case 1. Lastly, in Figure 6.3c the computation time when
using 15 POD bases, which corresponds to a accuracy of around 10−8 in case 2 and 10−5 in case 1,
is compared to the computation time for the high-ﬁdelity solver in case 1. It is clear that we see a
signiﬁcant speed-up.
Besides the measurements of performance, it is very interesting to see the actual POD basis functions
for both cases. In Figure 6.4 the ﬁrst six basis functions for case 1 are shown and in Figure 6.5, the
ﬁrst six for case 2 are shown. Here, it is quite obvious that the POD basis is computed on two diﬀerent
training sets. In case 1 the basis had to capture dynamics of a varying location of a heat source, while
in case 2 this was not necessary.

6.3
Viscous Burgers’ Equation
The viscous Burgers’ equation is given by
∂ty(µ) −ϵ∂xxy(µ) + y(µ)∂xy(µ) = 0,
in
Q,
(6.8a)
y(x, t;µ) = 0,
on
Σ,
(6.8b)
y(x, 0;µ) = y0(µ),
in
Ω,
(6.8c)
where ϵ ≪1. In the limit ϵ = 0, (6.8) is called the inviscid Burgers’ equation. Q denotes a space-time
cylinder. When y ∈W(0, T) and y0 ∈H1
0(Ω) the well-posedness of (6.8) is shown in [TV01].
The viscous Burgers’ equation is an advection-diﬀusion equation, with a nonlinear advection term. It
can be used as a model for various phenomena such as ﬂuids, acoustics, traﬃc ﬂow, etc. However,
in this thesis we consider it due to it’s interesting solution space. Namely the fact that for ϵ small
enough the solutions will be advection dominated and shocks will develop. In the context of reduced
basis methods it is interesting to see if the reduced basis can capture this dynamic eﬀectively.
The corresponding weak form of (6.8) is:
⟨∂ty(t;µ), ϕ⟩+ ϵ ⟨∇y(t;µ), ∇ϕ⟩+ ⟨y(t;µ)∂xy(t;µ), ϕ⟩= 0,
∀ϕ ∈V
(6.9a)
⟨y(0;µ), ϕ⟩= 0,
∀ϕ ∈V,
(6.9b)
or similarly stated as
⟨∂ty(t;µ), ϕ⟩+ F(y(t,µ), ϕ;µ) = 0,
∀ϕ ∈V
(6.10a)
⟨y(0;µ), ϕ⟩= ⟨y0(µ), ϕ⟩,
∀ϕ ∈V,
(6.10b)
with
F(y(t,µ), ϕ;µ) = ϵ ⟨∇y(t;µ), ∇ϕ⟩+ ⟨y(t;µ)∂xy(t;µ), ϕ⟩.
Discretizing in time using the θ-method gives the following nonlinear problem:
G(y(tk;µ), ϕ;µ) = 1
∆t ⟨y(tk;µ) −y(tk−1;µ), ϕ⟩+ θF(y(tk,µ), ϕ;µ) + (1 −θ)F(y(tk−1,µ), ϕ;µ) = 0.
(6.11)
By using Newtons method, we then get the following linear problem to be solved for δy = y(tk;µ) −
yn(tk;µ) in each Newton iteration:
DyG(yn(tk;µ), ϕ;µ)δy = −G(yn(tk;µ), ).
(6.12)
==PAGE:95==
6.3 Viscous Burgers’ Equation
81
Figure 6.9: Initial conditions for the viscous Burgers’ equation.
Solving (6.12) until a desired accuracy gives the next time iterate. Thus we need to solve multiple
linear problems for each time step.
We will speciﬁcally consider the case with Ω= [−1, 1] and with end time T = 1.6037/π. Furthermore,
we consider the initial condition
y0(µ) = −a sin(πx),
a > 0,
which is plotted for various a-values in Figure 6.9. With the diﬀusion parameter, ϵ, and the parameter,
a, in the initial condition, P is two-dimensional.
For the solution of the high-ﬁdelity problem we used FEniCS for the spatial discretizations and the Gâ-
teuax derivatives of the nonlinear form to derive the Jacobian, as explained in Section A. In particular
we used ﬁrst order ﬁnite elements with 512 degrees of freedom and 50 time steps. For the snapshots,
we varied the diﬀusion parameter, ϵ, between 0.001 and 0.1 and the initial amplitude between 0.5 and
1.5. Furthermore, we computed 50 snapshots for the computation of the POD basis.
In Figure 6.10a we see the convergence of the POD solution towards the high-ﬁdelity solution. There
is a clear spectral convergence, which suggests that we can represent the solution with a few number
of basis functions. This is further supported by Figure 6.10c where we see that much of the energy
is contained in the ﬁrst few singular values. More surprisingly is the signiﬁcant speed-up, when using
20 POD functions, which corresponds to an accuracy of around 10−10, shown in Figure 6.10b. As
mentioned in Section 4.4, the oﬄine and the online phase is not completely decoupled. We need to
assemble the high-ﬁdelity jacobian as well as the right hand side and project them onto the POD space
in each newton iteration. However, since we only have to solve a system of a much smaller size than
in the high-ﬁdelity case, we obtain a speed-up.
When looking at Figure 6.12a we see that the actual dynamics is highly focused in a rather small
spatial area around x = 0. This is the reason why we can represent the solution with such few basis
functions. It is, especially apparent in Figure 6.11a where we see the ﬁrst ﬁve basis functions. Most of
the oscillations are focused around x = 0 as expected. Furthermore, we see in Figure 6.11b that the
ﬁrst two basis functions have a much more signiﬁcant inﬂuence on the solution for most time instances.
This is not surprising considering that these two basis functions resemble the actual solution.

Chapter 7
Test Case: Optimal Control of
Nonlinear PDEs
In this chapter we will showcase the previously presented methods, namely the POD-SQP and the
POD-SQP-NN method. The test cases are optimal control of a nonlinear reaction-diﬀusion equation
as well as the viscous Burgers’ equation - both time dependent. For both equations we consider the
space-time cylinder Q = [0, 1] × [0, T] and two diﬀerent settings:
1. Distributed control with unconstrained control function.
2. Subdomain control with constrained control function.
We will refer to the ﬁrst one as the distributed control problem and the second one as the constrained
control problem. Furthermore, we will study two diﬀerent desired states:
• Case 1: Retain the initial condition given by
y0(x) =
(
h,
for ω < x < 1 −ω,
0,
otherwise.
See Figure 7.1a for an example of the desired state.
• Case 2: For initial condition y0(x) = 0 reach desired state given by
yd(x, t) =
(
h,
for ω < t < 2ωT
0,
otherwise.
See Figure 7.1b.
The reasons for choosing these two cases are quite diﬀerent and thereby has the potential to showcase
advantages as well as disadvantages of the considered methods. Both cases are interesting from an
application point of view. Case 1 because it is often of interest to maintain a certain state throughout
a timespan and case 2 because it is not unlikely that a state needs to be excited suddenly in time.
From a mathematical standpoint, the two cases complement each other quite well. The ﬁrst one has
variation in space and the second one in time. Since we are only computing reduced basis functions
in space it is interesting to see if this will aﬀect the performance.

7.1.1
SQP Method
As for the ﬁrst order optimality conditions, we have already derived the linear system to be solved in
each SQP iteration for the general case. Hence, for the equation under consideration we can simply

∂ty −ϵ∂xxy + 3(yn)2y −1

We use the linear ﬁnite element method with 200 elements in space and 75 time steps. We solve the
problem on the spatial domain Ω= [0, 1] and to T = 1 in time and choose β = 10−3.
Results for case 1 for varying parameter values are shown in Figure D.1 in Appendix D. We see
that the state is, more or less, retained unless the diﬀusion parameter is too large. This is due to
the fact that choosing β = 10−3 gives the control function the opportunity to adjust to the problem
without increasing the object functional by much. One sees that the control is changing quite a bit
==PAGE:100==
86
Test Case: Optimal Control of Nonlinear PDEs
for the various cases but retain the same overall shape. As expected, smaller ϵ results in a control
function with sharper edges and larger oscillations, since the nonlinear term dominates.
Results for case 2 for varying parameter values are shown in Figure D.2 in appendix D. Again, the
state is quite close to the desired state for all cases. In contrast to case 1, however, the control function
does not vary by much with the diﬀusion parameter. This is due to the fact that no matter how much
the diﬀusion term dominates, the control needs to create a pulse and shot it oﬀalmost immediately
afterwards. Hence, the diﬀusion in space does not play a large role and thereby, does not aﬀect the
necessary control.
In Table C.1 and C.2 in Appendix C we see that the number of SQP iterations is constant for varying
diﬀusion values.
7.1.2
Reduced Basis and Neural Network Approach
For the reduced order models we vary the following parameters:
• ϵ ∈[0.001, 0.1] - describing the amount of diﬀusion.
• h ∈[0.5, 1] - the height of the initial condition in case 1 and the height of the pulse in case 2.
• ω ∈[0.25, 0.4] - the width of the initial condition and the pulse in case 1 and case 2 respectively.
Thus, we deﬁne µ = (ϵ, h, w) ∈[0.001, 0.1] × [0.5, 1] × [0.25, 0.4]. In both case 1 and 2 we use 300
snapshots to generate the transformation matrices.
For the errors we use the relative l2 error:
Erb = ||¯yδ −W¯yrb||l2

7.1.2.1
POD-SQP
We follow the approach described in section 4.5. The parameter samples are sampled using the latin
hypercube sampling strategy.
It is worth mentioning that the number of SQP iterations is the same as for the high-ﬁdelity method
no matter how many POD functions we use. This is shown in Table C.1 and C.2 in Appendix C.
Case 1
In Figure 7.3a we see the convergence of the POD towards the high-ﬁdelity solution. As expected,
the POD-SQP errors are larger than or equal to the projection errors, as the projection is the best
possible approximation in the POD space. To get an error in the order of 10−7 for the state and the
adjoint state we need around 50 POD functions. In general, the error for the adjoint state is larger
than for the state. This is probably due to the more complicated solution space. This is also apparent
in Figure 7.3b where we see that the eigenvalues of the correlation matrix decreases faster for the state
than for the adjoint state. It is interesting to see that the eigenvalues decrease rather slowly until
==PAGE:101==
7.1 Optimal Control of a Reaction-Diﬀusion Equation
87
(a) Convergence of the POD.
(b) Eigenvalues of the correlation

approximately the 40th eigenvalue where they start to decrease rapidly. In Figure 7.3a we see that
the convergence rate increases at the same time. This is a clear visual representation of Proposition
4.3.
Even though we need fewer basis functions to get an accuracy around 10−7, than for the high-ﬁdelity
solution, we don’t get a speed-up, due to the necessity of transforming to the high-ﬁdelity space in
each iteration. This is shown in Figure 7.3c where we see that the computation time is around the
same for the two methods. Thus, the speed-up obtained from decreasing the size of the saddle point
system does not compensate for the necessity of transforming back and forth from the high-ﬁdelity
space.
Furthermore, in Figure E.1 in Appendix E, we see the ﬁrst ﬁve POD basis functions for the state as
well as for the adjoint state. Unsurprisingly, we see that the basis functions resemble the solutions by
having ﬂuctuations in the areas of the domain where most of the dynamic is focused.
Case 2
In Figure 7.4a we see the convergence of the POD towards the high-ﬁdelity solution. Again, the POD-
SQP errors are larger than the projection errors. The POD-SQP method converges much faster for
this case than for case 1. This is due to the fact that while the variations in ω aﬀected the spatial width
of the initial condition in case 1, the variations in ω aﬀects the temporal location of the pulse in case
2. Thus, there is less variation in space, which in turn results in a need for fewer basis functions. We
already see an error in the order of magnitude 10−7 around 20 POD functions. This behavior is also
apparent in ﬁgure 7.4b where we see that the eigenvalues decrease faster than for case 1. Furthermore,
the lower dimensional POD space results in actual speed-ups. This is shown in Figure 7.4c where we
see speed-ups of around 4 to 6 times for the varying samples.
In Figure E.2 in Appendix E we see the basis functions for both the state and the adjoint state have
most of their ﬂuctuations around the boundary.
7.1.2.2
POD-SQP-NN
To simplify the analysis and be able to compare performance we used the same number of training,
validation, and test samples for the two cases. We used 1800 training samples, 100 validation samples,
and 100 test samples. The validation set was used for early stopping by measuring the error every 5
epoch. When 10 measurements in a row showed increase in validation error we stopped the training.
Furhermore, we used l2 regularization in the loss function.
To set hyperparameters we used cross validation as described in section 5.3. We tuned the learning
==PAGE:102==
88
Test Case: Optimal Control of Nonlinear PDEs
(a) Convergence of the POD.
(b) Eigenvalues of the correnlation
matrix for case 2.
(c)
Computation time using 20
POD basis functions.
Figure 7.4: Convergence,eigenvalues and computation time for y3-equation case 2. We see a 5-7
times speed-up. This is due to the fact, that for case 2 we don’t need many POD basis functions.
rate as well as the regularization parameter. We found that neither made a big impact since gener-
alization was not really an issue. However, the regularization parameter had to be chosen relatively
small. We used λ = 0.0001 throughout the analysis. The learning rate did not aﬀect the outcome
of the training but merely the time it took to train unless it was chosen too large, which resulted in
oscillating loss. We used α = 0.001 for the ﬁnal training.
For the optimization we employed the Adam optimizer with β1 = 0.9 and β2 = 0.999.
We used
mini-batches of size 32 as this was found to be a good size through trial-and-error. The weights are
initialized by a standard Gaussian distribution.
For the network structure we tested networks with two hidden layers and three hidden layers before
settling on three. In this thesis, we only show results for three hidden layers as they, in general,
performed best. Furthermore, we imposed the same number of neurons, H, in each hidden layer and
we added bias terms in every layer.
We tested various activation functions and found that the results did not vary signiﬁcantly. In general,
the leaky ReLU activation function showed slightly better results. Thus, all the results shown in this
chapter are trained on networks with the Leaky ReLU as the activation function in every neuron
except for the output layer where the identity activation function was chosen.
We used PyTorch and it’s various modules, described in Section B, for the programming of the neural
networks.
In the testing phase we trained networks on both the state and the control and found very simi-
lar results for the two. To simplify the presentation of results, we only show results for the control
functions.
Plots of neural network computed responses and errors compared to the high-ﬁdelity solutions can be
found in Appendix F.
For errors we use the relative l2 error:
Enn = ||¯yδ −W¯ynn||l2
||¯yδ||l2
.
(7.6)
The errors were computed on the test sets and averaged.
For the training of the many networks we utilized DTU HPC infrastructure. See [DTU] for more
details. With the cluster set-ups we could train networks on multiple CPUs at once, which signiﬁ-
cantly sped up the training phase.
To test how the number of time steps, NT , and the varying parameters aﬀected the eﬀectiveness
of the networks we will show convergence results for both NT = 75 with end time T = 1 and NT = 20
with end time T = 0.2. Furthermore, we tested these two time settings for constant ω, that is the width
of the initial condition in case 1 and the placement and width of the pulse in case 2, as well as varying ω.

the analysis and discussion on how precise and reliable the POD-SQP-NN method is.
Case 1
The convergence results of case 1 are shown in Figure 7.6.
In all cases, we see improvements in
accuracy when increasing the number of neurons in each layer as expected. Though one sees that
this tendency stops at a certain point where increasing the number of neurons does not increase the
accuracy.
Probably due to either insuﬃcient training, insuﬃcient number of training samples, or
something else. It should also be noted that the convergence curves ﬂuctuates quite a lot, which
implies that the weight optimization could have been improved upon further.
In all cases the errors are bounded below by the projection errors and the NN errors actually follow
that curve quite close until a certain number of POD basis functions are required. Here, the NN errors
stagnate.
When using few time steps NT = 20, we get a higher precision than in the long time cases. This is,
again, not surprising due to the simpler solution space and fewer trainable parameters and a much
smaller target space. However, in the simpler cases with constant ω = 0.3 the accuracy is actually
more or less the same for the long time case as for the short time case. It is admittedly slightly
disappointing only to get an error around 10−2 in the best cases.
In Figure F.1 we see that the solutions, computed by the neural networks, and the high-ﬁdelity method
respectively, are qualitatively very diﬃcult to tell apart. By this we mean that when looking at the
solutions side by side it is very diﬃcult to see any diﬀerences. Furthermore, the networks are actually
able to adjust the output from being very smooth to almost discontinuous at a few spatial points for
the varying ϵ values. But with a closer look at the errors, it is obvious that the neural network solution
is oﬀ. As expected the errors are larger for small ϵ values where the nonlinearity dominates. Here the
errors are focused around the ﬂuctuations in the control function.

Case 2
The convergence results for case 2 are shown in Figure 7.7. The general behavior is very similar to

varying ω, in Figure 7.7b there is close to no improvement when adding neurons and POD functions,
while when keeping ω constant, we see in Figure 7.7d that we get an error just below 10−2. For the
short time cases there is not much diﬀerence from the constant ω cases compared to the varying ω
cases. We do, however, get an error down to 10−3.
The higher accuracy, compared to case 1, could come from the fact that much fewer POD basis
functions are needed in case 2 to achieve higher accuracy. As we see in Figure 7.4a.

SQP Method
As for the ﬁrst order optimality conditions, we have already derived the linear system to be solved in

where the solution diﬀuses. Furthermore, we see that the control function is varying a lot with the
parameters. For small diﬀusion parameter, that is when the ﬂow is advection dominated, the control
has very sharp edges. It is also worth noting that the control is not symmetric due to the fact that it
tries to limit the advection in one direction.
In Figure D.4 in appendix D, we see solutions for case 2. As for the y3-equation the state resem-

for varying parameters. Again, this is due to the fact that the value of the state is brought down
before it advects or diﬀuses.
In Table C.3 and C.4 in Appendix C we see that in contrast to the y3-equation the number of SQP
iterations varies a lot with the diﬀusion parameter. This could be a sign that the method chosen is
not very well-suited for nonlinear advection dominated problems. We will return to this discussion in
Chapter 9.

For the reduced order models we vary the following parameters:
• ϵ ∈[0.001, 0.1] - the amount of diﬀusion.
• h ∈[0.5, 1] - the height of the initial condition in case 1 and the height of the pulse in case 2.
• w ∈[0.25, 0.4] - the width of the initial condition and the pulse in case 1 and case 2 respectively.

snapshots to generate the transformation matrices.

7.2.3.1
POD-SQP
We see in Appenix C that the number of SQP iterations is, more or less, the same for increasing
number of POD functions for ϵ = 0.1 and ϵ = 0.01. While still being slightly higher than for the
high-ﬁdelity method. However, for ϵ = 0.001 we see that the number of necessary iterations varies
as we vary the number of POD functions and the method uses signiﬁcantly more iterations than the
high-ﬁdelity method. This could be a sign that the POD-SQP method moves towards instability as
the problem becomes more and more advection dominated.
Case 1
In Figure 7.9a we see the convergence of the POD-SQP method. While the convergence is of spectral
order, it is still not as fast as for the y3-equation. We need around 90 POD basis to get an accuracy
around 10−5, which is much worse than for the y3-equation. The reason is the more complicated
solution space due to the nonlinear advection in Burgers’ equation. This also results is much slower
computation times. In Figure 7.9c we actually see that the POD-SQP method is slower than the
high-ﬁdelity method for all test samples while only getting an accuracy around 10−4. This is the exact
reason why we want to look at other methods to obtain a fast online model.
Case 2
Unfortunately, the POD-SQP method did not converge as shown in ﬁgure 7.10a, even though the
projection error converges quite rapidly. Hence, the fault must be found in the Galerkin or Newton
part of the method. It is not uncommon that the POD-Galerkin system is unstable even though the
high-ﬁdelity counter part is [Ubb17], [QMN15]. We will discuss methods to solve this issue in Chapter
9.

that we only go down to ϵ = 0.01 instead of 0.001 for Burgers’ equation as in the previous cases. This is

Furthermore, we have decreased the number of time steps down to 30 for both cases, as well as the
time T down to 0.5 for the y3-equation and 0.3 for Burgers’ equation.
7.3.2.1
POD-SQP
Firstly, it should be mentioned that we did not manage to make the POD-SQP method converge for
Burgers’ equation. The reasons could be many, but we hypothesize that it probably has to do with
the following:

exponentially but then slows down and only decreases with polynomial order.
For the y3-equation we get very decent results when using the POD-SQP method. In Figure 7.14a

there is quite a large diﬀerence between the state error and the adjoint state error and the curves are
not decreasing as smoothly.
Regarding computation time, there is a speed-up between 20% and 30% for all samples.

7.4
Varying the Size of the Training Set
In all of the above examples we have kept the number of training samples constant. Since the training
set is quite important for the training of a neural network we will brieﬂy discuss how the number of
samples aﬀect the accuracy of the POD-SQP-NN method.
In Figure (7.18) we see convergence plots for varying training sets for the simplest case and for the
most diﬃcult case for Burgers’ equation case 1. As expected, a larger training set results in a higher
accuracy for higher POD dimension. However, the improvement is not by much, which points to the
fact that we need more than simply more training data to achieve better results.

==PAGE:117==
Chapter 8
Conclusion
For convenience we state the main objective again:
Assess the potential of using reduced order models and artiﬁcial neural networks as re-
placements for traditional scientiﬁc computing elements in nonlinear PDE-constrained
optimization.
Regarding the reduced basis approaches we conclude partial success. In Chapter 6 we saw that the
POD-Galerkin method decreased the computation time for both the 2D linear case, the 1D reaction-
advection-diﬀusion equation with Robin BC, and even for the nonlinear viscous Burgers’ equation
without compromising accuracy. This showed the potential of the reduced basis approach for solving
various dynamic PDEs.
However, when adding the control aspect the POD approach did not show as promising results. Only in
one case (y3-equation case 2) did we see a signiﬁcant speed-up by employing the POD-SQP method.
Furthermore, for some cases involving Burgers’ equation the POD-SQP method did not converge.
These problems will brieﬂy be discussed in the next chapter on future work.
Despite the modest success of the POD-SQP method we were still able to identify lower dimensional
reduced spaces in which we could project the solution onto. This was concluded based on the conver-
gence of the projection errors in all cases. We utilized this in the POD-SQP-NN procedure.
For the neural networks we did manage to successfully implement the procedure presented in Chapter
5.4 using FEniCS and PyTorch. While the online computations were signiﬁcantly faster than for the
POD-SQP-NN and the high-ﬁdelity method, the accuracy was slightly disappointing. The networks
were able to capture the overall dynamics for the various parameter values, but were not able to
approximate better than around 10−2 and 10−3 depending on the case. Furthermore, we saw that the
choice of parameters to vary aﬀected the approximation abilities quite a lot for the networks. Here,
we speciﬁcally refer to parameter ω. It is, however, worth noting that there were diﬀerences between
the cases where variations were primarily in space (case 1) and the ones where variations primarily
were in time (case 2). In general, did the POD-SQP-NN method, as well as the POD-SQP method,
perform slightly better in case 2 where the variations were primarily in time. This did not come as a
surprise since fewer POD bases were necessary to approximate the high-ﬁdelity solutions.
Lastly, we need to emphasize that the errors varied quite a lot with the number of neurons and POD
functions. Theoretically the errors should decrease monotonically, but in practice, probably due to
insuﬃcient training, the errors ﬂuctuated a lot. We saw that the number of training samples did
inﬂuence the eﬀectiveness of the training, but not by much.
We conclude that the POD-SQP-NN method has potential to replace traditional elements in PDE-
constrained optimization, but is, at the current state not there yet. The accuracy must be lower to
utilize the method for serious practical applications and it deﬁnitely needs to be more reliable. That
==PAGE:118==
104
Conclusion
being said, as a ﬁrst approach, to the best of our knowledge, of implementing all-at-once solvers for
dynamical PDE-constrained optimization using neural networks, we were able to capture the rather
complicated dynamics of the control functions for both the distributed unconstrained control case and
the subdomain constrained control cases. Which points to the fact that with more study, trial-and-
error and more advanced techniques it could be possible to create a more accurate and reliable method
based on the ﬁndings in this thesis.
==PAGE:119==
Chapter 9
Future Work
We saw in Section 7 that we only got moderate results and therefore were restricted to conclude only
partial success. However, the potentials of the SQP-POD and the SQP-POD-NN method are present,
but the methods need to be reﬁned. In this chapter we will give some ideas as to what to do in future
work.
Improving POD-SQP
When analyzing the POD-SQP method we found that for some instances the method did not converge,
but only when dealing with Burgers’ equation. Therefore, we propose methods to solve this issue and
make the method more reliable.
As wee see it, there are two places where it could go wrong: 1) In the Galerkin formulation of the
linear system to be solved in each SQP system (3.20), or 2) in the Newton algorithm.
Improved Galerkin Methods
It is well-known that even though the high-ﬁdelity problem is stable the stability is not necessar-
ily ensured in the POD-G framework. When it comes to saddle-point problems, one would need to
stabilize the system [QMN15]. The stability of a saddle point system is ensured by an inf-sup con-
dition and in cases of instability there are various methods to stabilize the system. For a thorough
introduction to saddle point problems see [Luo06] and in context of reduced basis methods, see [GV12].
Besides looking at problems regarding the saddle-point formulation one could look into better ways to
deal with the advection term in Burgers’ equation. We simply used an implicit Euler method, which
is unconditionally stable, but need a lot of time steps and is deﬁnitely not the best approach. To
this end, one could look into higher order methods or space-time element formulations. Furthermore,
it is well-known that the Galerkin method i not the best suited to deal with advection in space. It
does work if one chooses enough elements. However, to stabilize the scheme and get better results
with fewer elements on could employ Petrov-Galerkin methods [QQ09], [KH15], [CJ18] or even better
employ discontinuous Galerkin methods [HW07]. While these approaches have shown to be better
they might require more work to put into a bigger all-at-once solver framework.
==PAGE:120==
106
Future Work
Improved SQP/Newton Method
Firstly, for dealing with the nonlinearities we used the Newton/SQP method. This method is known
to be only a local solver. Thus one is not ensured to converge to a minimum if a proper initial guess is
not chosen. One could combine the Newton approach with a gradient method to get a global scheme
and in that way increase the chance of converging.
Secondly, we used the semi-smooth Newton method for the non-smooth case (the control constrained
case). Another approach to deal with this problem is the so-called primal-dual active set strategy
[TV01], [Trö10]. Utilizing this method might decrease both the computation time as well as increase
the chance of successfully converge. The eﬀectiveness of this is, however, not known at this point when
it is utilized together with a reduced basis POD scheme.
Improving POD-SQP-NN
The POD-SQP-NN scheme was shown to be much faster in the online phase than the other methods.
Unfortunately, one had to compromise on accuracy. For the method to be of any serious practical use,
this needs to be ﬁxed.
we saw that increasing the number of training only improved the accuracy by very little. Hence, we
must look into other ways of improving the method.
Space-Time Discretization
When analyzing the convergence plots in chapter 7 it became apparent that the number of time steps
aﬀected the accuracy quite a lot. This motivates putting more eﬀort into minimizing the number of
degrees of freedom in the time direction. With a ﬁnite diﬀerence scheme in the time direction, we are
not able to decrease the time dimension with reduced basis methods. This could be ﬁxed by utilizing
space-time Galerkin methods, where one represents the solution by basis functions in both time and
space [CJ18]:
y(x, t) =
Nx
X
i=1
Nt
X
j=1
ˆyi,jϕi(x)ωj(t).
(9.1)
With this high-ﬁdelity discretization one would then be able to identify reduced basis functions in both
time and space and thereby decrease the number of degrees of freedom signiﬁcantly. Furthermore,
the actual properties, i.e. the physics of the solution space, will be apparent in both time and space,
which means that more physical properties will be incorporated into the neural network approach.
Recurrent Neural Networks
Instead of decreasing the dimension by space-time methods, one could try to implement a network
architecture better suited for time dependent data.
We are speciﬁcally thinking about recurrent
neural networks (RNN) [Hay94]. RNNs have shown to be better suited for dealing with temporal data
[SSB14], [MC01].
==PAGE:121==
107
Neural Ordinary Diﬀerential Equations
Another very new approach is using Neural Ordinary Diﬀerential Equations also called ODE-nets
[CRBD19]. The idea starts with residual neural networks, where the input to the next layer is both
the activation output of the previous layer as well as the identity output. Hence, the state of a neuron,
h, at time t + 1 is given by
ht+1 = ht + f(ht, θt).
(9.2)
Here, one could realize that this is oddly familiar to an Euler discretization. Thus, in the continuous
limit of having an inﬁnite amount of hidden layers, one could interpret (9.2) as a discretization of the
ODE
d
dth(t) = f(h(t), θt).
(9.3)
By utilizing this, one switches the discrete hidden layers with a system of ODEs. In [CRBD19] this
has shown to produce great results for approximating time dependent functions. Since a spatially
discretized PDE is a ﬁnite number of time dependent functions this idea could be utilized in the
framework presented in this thesis.
Long Finite Time Horizon Optimization
In all simulations the time horizon of the problems has been fairly short.
In practice, it is of-
ten necessary to be able to solve problems for longer time periods.
To this end, one could uti-
lize sub-optimal strategies.
The basic idea is to divide the time domain, [0, T], into sub domains
[0, T1], [T1, T2], . . . , [TN−1, T] to be solved for. After solving the problem on the ﬁrst subdomain one
uses the end state as the initial state for the next optimization problem and solve that on the next
domain. This continues until T is reached [Hei05]. This approach results in a suboptimal strategy
since one is not considering the full horizon in each optimization problem.
For the neural networks this means that one would have to train a network on, not only the parameters,
but also the entire initial state and then train the network to advance to a predeﬁned time.
==PAGE:122==
108
Future Work
==PAGE:123==
Appendix A
FEniCS Project
The FEniCS Project is a collection of free and open-source software for automated solving of diﬀer-
ential equations in Python and C [ABH+15]. It is designed as an umbrella project for a collection of
several components operating together. In total it amounts to a framework that is quite general and
convenient for solving diﬀerential equations based on weak formulations. We will in this section go
through the building blocks of a fairly simple and typical FEniCS block of code to solve the Poisson
equation in 2D.
In the FEniCS framework, one ﬁrstly has to deﬁne a mesh on a domain. There are several predeﬁned
domains for mesh creation. Below is an example of a rectangular domain:
#Domain :
Rectangle

−2), Point (2 ,
2) , nx ,
ny)
After the domain and the mesh are deﬁned, one has to deﬁne the function space on the domain. Here,
one has to choose which type of elements one wants to use and what order:
V = FunctionSpace (mesh ,
’P ’ ,
order )
P denotes standard Lagrangian elements. Next the boundary subdomains must be deﬁned such that
one can apply boundary conditions. Below is an example of homogeneous Dirichlet boundary condi-
tions:

space V :
u = TrialFunction (V)

onto the space V :

integrate an expression one has to multiply by an integration measure dx. The weak form is thereby
deﬁned in the following way:

Above is the weak form a(u, ϕ) −f(ϕ) deﬁned, and thereafter divided into the left and the right hand
side. The variable, a, corresponds to the matrix (2.31) and and, L, corresponds to the vector f in
(2.30). Then to solve the system one has to deﬁne a function on the space V in which we can save the
solution when using solve together with the boundary condition:
u_solution = Function (V)
solve ( a == L,
u_solution ,
bc )
By applying the boundary conditions to the function call, FEniCS modiﬁes the matrix A as well as
the right hand side, such that the boundary conditions are fulﬁlled.
If it is of interest to actually get the matrices and vectors instead of solving them within the FEniCS
framework, one can get them as NumPy arrays in the following way
#Left
hand
side
A_numpy = assembly ( a )
bc . apply (A_numpy)
A_numpy = A_numpy. array ()
#Right hand
side
L_numpy = assembly (L)
bc . apply (L_numpy)
L_numpy = A_numpy. get_local ()
This constitutes a fast way to e.g. build a mass matrix:
#Mass matrix
M_form = u∗v∗dx
M_numpy = assembly (M_form)
bc . apply (M_numpy)
M_numpy = M_numpy. array ()
Similar approaches can be used to deﬁne other more complicated system matrices.
If one has data as a vector, e.g. an initial condition or a right hand side, it is not possible to simply
multiply FEniCS functions by these to include them in the weak forms. However, it is possible to
deﬁne a function and then impose that the nodal values should be that of the vector:
f = Function (V)
f . vector ( ) [ : ] = vector
For time dependent problems one would deﬁne a function to keep the data from the previous time
step and then iteratively update that:
==PAGE:125==
111
#Define
t e s t
and
t r i a l
functions
v = TestFunction (V)
u = TrialFunction (V)
#Define
i n i t i a l
condition
u_0 = Expression ( ’ exp(−b∗pow(x [ 0 ] ,
2) −b∗pow(x [ 1 ] ,
2)) ’ , degree =2,b=b)
#Define
previous
i t e r a t e
function
u_old = Function (V)
u_old = i n t e r p o l a t e (u_0 ,V)
#Define weak form
using
the
theta
method
theta = 0.5
F = (u −u_old )∗v∗dx + theta ∗dt ∗( kappa∗dot ( grad (u ) ,
grad (v )) −f ∗v)∗dx \
+ (1. −theta )∗dt ∗( kappa∗dot ( grad ( u_old ) ,
grad (v )) −f ∗v)∗dx
a , L = lhs (F) ,
rhs (F)
#Define
function
to
save
s o l u t i o n
in
u_sol = Function (V)
#Step
forward
in
time
f o r
t
in
range ( num_steps ) :
t += dt
#Compute
s o l u t i o n
solve ( a == L,
u_sol ,
bc )
#Update
previous
s o l u t i o n
u_old . assign ( u_sol )
Note that we have assumed that the number of time steps is deﬁned earlier in the script. Lastly we
will show that FEniCS can handle nonlinear problems as well. Given a nonlinear weak form, G, is
deﬁned in the framework, one can then solve the problem in the following way:
solve (G == 0 , u_sol ,
bc )
Or one can compute the Jacobian, using the derivative function, evaluated in a previous iterate u_k
and then solve the linear system:
Du = Function (V)
DG = d e r i v a t i v e (G, u_k,Du)
solve (DG == −G,
u_sol ,
bc )
We will, in general use FEniCS to construct matrices and to a lesser degree the solver capabilities.
