FEniCS Project
==PAGE:127==
Appendix B
Implementing Neural Networks in
PyTorch
As we used FEniCS to handle the ﬁnite element discretizations we will use the framework PyTorch
to handle many of the technical diﬃculties when it comes to implementations of neural networks.
PyTorch is an open-source machine learning library for Python. It consists of various modules, three
of which we will use extensively in our code:
• Autograd Module. Implementations of various automatic diﬀerentiation procedures, including
back propagation [PGC+17].
• Optim Module. Implementations of various optimization algorithms, including Adam [KB14].
• nn Module. This module makes it easy and intuitive to build deep neural networks. It packages
the functionalities of the Optim and Autograd module into an easy-to-program set-up.
The primary data structure used in PyTorch is the tensor. It is quite similar to a NumPy array but
with the potential of strong GPU acceleration built in.
The easiest way of describing how to build a neural network using PyTorch is to show it step by step.
One starts by deﬁning a network class. Inside the class one deﬁnes the layers by choosing whether it
should be a linear propagation function or e.g. a convolutional layer. Furthermore, one must deﬁne the
number of inputs and outputs in the given layer as well as whether there should be a bias term. Next
one must deﬁne which activation function (if any) one wants to use. Lastly, the forward propagation
must be deﬁned by combining all the previous components. Below is an example of a network with
three hidden layers and an output layer using the leaky ReLU activation function for each layer except
for the output where the identity activation function is used. Note that the input layer is not counted
as a layer.
c l a s s
Net (nn . module )
def __init__( s e l f ) :
super ( s e l f ) . __init__ ()
#Layer 1
s e l f . l_1=Linear ( in_features=input_size ,
out_features=num_neurons , bias=True )
#Layer 2
s e l f . l_2=Linear ( in_features=num_neurons ,
==PAGE:128==
114
Implementing Neural Networks in PyTorch
out_features=num_neurons , bias=True )
#Layer 3
s e l f . l_3=Linear ( in_features=num_neurons ,
out_features=num_neurons , bias=True )
#Layer 4
s e l f . l_4=Linear ( in_features=input_size ,
out_features=output_size , bias=True )
#Activation
function :
Leaky ReLU
s e l f . a c t i v a t i o n = torch . nn . LeakyReLU ()
#Forward
propagation
def
forward ( s e l f ,x)
x = s e l f . l_1 (x)
x = s e l f . a c t i v a t i o n (x)
x = s e l f . l_2 (x)
x = s e l f . a c t i v a t i o n (x)
x = s e l f . l_3 (x)
x = s e l f . a c t i v a t i o n (x)
x = s e l f . l_4 (x)
return x
To train a network for a speciﬁc purpose one must now create an instance of the network class. There
is a default weight initialization, but it is possible to initialize the weights using other predeﬁned
initializations or even deﬁne one from scratch. This is done by deﬁning a weight initialization procedure
and then apply it to the network instance. Next, a loss function must be chosen. The most common
ones are implemented in the nn module, but it is possible to deﬁne one for a speciﬁc purpose. Before
the actual training starts, one must lastly choose which optimizer to use. Again, the most common
ones are already implemented in the Optim module. Below is an example of creating an instance of
the Net class, initializing the weights using a normalized Gaussian, deﬁning an MSE loss function and
using the Adam optimizer:
net = Net ()
def
weights_init (m) :
i f
i s i n s t a n c e (m,
nn . Linear ) :
nn . i n i t . normal_ (m. weight . data , mean=0, std =1)
i f
np . shape (m. bias ) !=
( ) :
nn . i n i t . normal_ (m. bias . data , mean=0, std =1)
net . apply ( weights_init )
l o s s = nn . MSELoss ()
optimizer = torch . optim .Adam( net . parameters ( ) ,
l r=learning_rate ,
weight_decay=r e g u l a r i z a t i o n )
For the Adam optimizer there are various parameters to be set. Above we set the learning rate and
l2 regularization parameters (called weight decay in PyTorch).
For the actual training, one must
ﬁrst input the training features and do a forward propagation. Then compute the loss and then do
a backward propagation. After that, one can take a step using the chosen optimizer. Below is an
example where a full batch is used:
f o r
e
in
range (num_epochs ) :
epoch_output = net ( X_train . f l o a t ( ) ) #Forward
propagation
epoch_loss = l o s s ( epoch_output ,
Y_train . f l o a t ( ) ) #Loss
==PAGE:129==
115
epoch_loss . backward () #Backward
propagation
optimizer . step () #Optimizer
step
Here we see the strength and convenience in using PyTorch. The forward and backward propagation
as well as the loss and optimizer are just a couple of functions calls. PyTorch ensures the eﬃcient back
propagation by saving the necessary values along the way. Furthermore, the optimizer remembers the
previous steps necessary for algorithm to be eﬀective, e.g. the moving average. Lastly, if one wants
to train on mini batches instead of the full data set it can be done by deﬁning a batch size and then
build a training loop inside the epoch loops to run through all the batches:
f o r
e
in
range (num_epochs ) :
permutation = torch . randperm ( X_train . s i z e ( ) [ 0 ] )
f o r
i
in
range (0 ,
X_train . s i z e ( ) [ 0 ] ,
batch_size ) :
optimizer . zero_grad ()
i n d i c e s = permutation [ i : i + batch_size ]
batch_x = X_train [ i n d i c e s ] . f l o a t ()
batch_y = Y_train [ i n d i c e s ] . f l o a t ()
batch_output = net ( batch_x )
batch_loss = l o s s ( batch_output ,
batch_y )
batch_loss . backward ()
optimizer . step ()
To implement early stopping one must include a forward propagation on the validation set in the loop
and break when a certain condition is met.

==PAGE:149==
Appendix G
Code Guide
All the code used in the thesis is collected in one .py ﬁle. Each component is packaged into functions
and can be called when the ﬁle is imported in a main script.
Below we will brieﬂy present each
function.
POD Functions
• POD(S,*args)
– Input: Snapshot matrix, S, and either: 1) the number of wanted POD bases, or 2) ’eps’,
followed by tolerance.
– Output: Transformation matrix W ∈RNδ×N.
• eigen(A)
– Input: Matrix A.
– Output: Eigenvalues ordered by size and matrix with eigenvectors as columns ordered
corresponding to the eigenvalues.
• LHS(xmin,xmax,nsample)
– Input: List the minimum values xmin, list the maximum values xmax, number of samples
nsample.
– Output: A matrix with each row, i, being a vector of generated values between xmin[i]
and xmax[i] based on the latin hypercube sampling.
Time Dependent PDE Functions
Snapshot Matrices
• createSnapshotMatrixHeat(u0, bc, f, V, mesh, mu, numsteps, T) - Heat equation
• createSnapshotMatrixBurger(V, mesh, bc, y0, f, muvec, numsteps, T) - Burgers’ equa-
tion
==PAGE:150==
136
Code Guide
• CreateLinearChromoSnapshot(V, mesh, a, epsilon, v, Nt, L, cf, tinj, T, numsteps,
muvec) - linear reaction-advection-diﬀusion equation
The above functions have the same input and output
• Input: Finite element space V in the FEniCS framework, and initial condition, boundary con-
ditions, a mesh deﬁned in the FEniCS framework, number of time steps, a matrix consisting of
parameter vectors, end time, right hand side, and constant parameters based on the problem.
• Output: The snapshot matrix
Other Functions
• get_dof_solution(u,mesh)
– Input: Function in FEniCS format and mesh in FEniCS format.
– Output: degrees of freedom for the function
• get_vertex_solution(u,mesh)
– Input: Function in FEniCS format and mesh in FEniCS format.
– Output: Vertex values for the function
• ProhectSystem(Adelta,fdelta,W)
– Input: High-ﬁdelity lhs, high-ﬁdelity rhs and transformation matrix.
– output: RB lhs, RB rhs
• ProhectSystemWithMass(Mdelta,Adelta,fdelta,W) - same as above but with mass matrix as
well
• SolveRBSystem(AN,FN, mu)
– Input: RB lhs, RB rhs, parameters
– Output: Solution to RB system
• SolveRBTimeStep(u_old, M_N, A_N, f_N, mu, dt)
– Input: Previous time solution, mass matrix, system matrix, rhs, parameters, step size - all
in RB space
– Output: Solution at next time step
Optimal Control Functions
High-Fidelity
• set_up_system(y_d, y_0, y_k, p_k, f, nu, Nt, n_space, dt,V, bc, beta, mesh,equation)
– Input: Previous iterate, desired state, β, time step size, diﬀusion coeﬃcient, ﬁnite element
space in FEniCS format, mech in FEniCS format, BC in FEniCS format, number of time
steps, number of spatial points, string denoting whether it is y3-equation or Burgers’
– Output: lhs and rhs for the saddle point problem.
==PAGE:151==
137
• burgers(N_space,N_time,mu_vec,T,beta,format,case)
– Input: Number of spatial points, number of time steps, parameter vector, end time, β, case
to be solved.
– Output: Optimal state and adjoint state for optimal control of Burgers’ equation.
• y_cubed(N_space,N_time,mu_vec,T,beta,format,case)
– Input: Number of spatial points, number of time steps, parameter vector, end time, β, case
to be solved.
– Output: Optimal state and adjoint state for optimal control of y3 equation.
• generate_snapshot_list(equation,case,N_space,N_time,mu_vec,T,beta)
– Input: Speciﬁcation of what equation to be considered and otherwise the same as burgers()
and y_cubed
– Output: List of optimal state and adjoint state of length speciﬁed by the size of the pa-
rameter vectors.
• set_up_desired_and_init(n_space, Nt, beta, nu, x, t_vec, h, w,case)
All the following functions set up the matrices for state or the adjoint state equations in the high-
ﬁdelity solvers:
• set_up_state_burgers(y_k, f, beta, dt, nu, V, bc, mesh) - Burgers’ state
• set_up_adjoint_burgers(y_d, y_k, p_k, f, beta, dt, nu, V, bc, mesh) - Burgers’ ad-
joint state
• set_up_state_y_cubed(y_k, f, beta, dt, nu, V, bc, mesh) - y3 state
• set_up_adjoint_y_cubed(y_d, y_k, p_k, f, beta, dt, nu, V, bc, mesh) - y3 adjoint state
They take and return the following
• Input: Previous iterate, β, time step size, diﬀusion coeﬃcient, ﬁnite element space in FEniCS
format, mech in FEniCS format, BC in FEniCS format. The adjoint state functions also take
the desired state.
• Output: The lhs matrix to multiply the state DOFs, the lhs matrix to multiply the adjoint state
DOFs, the rhs
POD-SQP Solvers
All functions here are similar to the high-ﬁdelity SQP solvers but also take the transformation matrices
as input and output the POD-SQP solutions
• set_up_system_POD(VV_Y,VV_P,y_d, y_0, y_k, p_k, f, nu, Nt, n_space, dt,V, bc, beta,
mesh,equation)
• burgers_POD(VV_Y,VV_P,N_space,N_time,mu_vec,T,beta,format,case)
• y_cubed_POD(VV_Y,VV_P,N_space,N_time,mu_vec,T,beta,format,case)
==PAGE:152==
138
Code Guide
The following functions take the same input and output the same as the high-ﬁdelity versions, but
slightly diﬀerent in order to ﬁt POD framework. Speciﬁcally the boundary conditions are not applied
since they are build into the POD functions.
• set_up_state_burgers_POD(y_k, f, beta, dt, nu, V, bc, mesh) - Burgers’ state
• set_up_adjoint_burgers_POD(y_d, y_k, p_k, f, beta, dt, nu, V, bc, mesh) - Burgers’
adjoint state
• set_up_state_y_cubed_(y_k, f, beta, dt, nu, V, bc, mesh) - y3 state
• set_up_adjoint_y_cubed_(y_d, y_k, p_k, f, beta, dt, nu, V, bc, mesh) - y3 adjoint state
SQP Solvers for Control Constrained Problem
All functions here do the same as for the high-ﬁdelity SQP and POD-SQP functions but with extra
inputs and outputs:
• Extra inputs: Subdomain where control is allowed, ua and ub.
• Extra outputs: The control function (since now the control function is not simply a scaling of
the adjoint state)
All the functions are:
• set_up_state_burgers_subdomain_constrained
• set_up_state_y_cubed_subdomain_constrained
• set_up_system_subdomain_constrained
• set_up_subdomain_and_constrained
• burgers_subdomain_constrained
• y_cubed_subdomain_constrained
• set_up_state_cubed_POD_subdomain_constrained
• set_up_adjoint_cubed_POD_subdomain_constrained
• set_up_state_burgers_POD_subdomain_constrained
• set_up_adjoint_burgers_POD_subdomain_constrained
• set_up_system_POD_subdomain_constrained
• burgers_POD_subdomain_constrained
• y_cubed_POD_subdomain_constrained
• H(P,beta,u_a,u_b)
– Input: adjoint state, β, ua and ub.
– Output: The projection of the adjoint state onto the admissible control space.

def __init__(self,activation_function,num_mu,num_neurons,net_output_size)

– Input: Activation function, number of input parameters, number of neurons in each layer
and output size.

state_or_adjoint,constrained)
– Implementation of algorithm

Appendix H
Project Plan and Evaluation
Project Plan
Project aim
We aim to show that it is possible to replace aspects of algorithms from scientiﬁc computing with
methods from deep learning. As speciﬁc examples, we will try to use artiﬁcial neural networks (ANNs)
as means to decouple the oﬄine and online phase in a reduced-basis approach to optimal control of
nonlinear PDE’s.
The aspiration, in the long term, is to signiﬁcantly speed-up online computations at the cost of
extending the oﬄine pre-processing stage to include training of the ANNs.
Outline
We will start by solving a linear time-dependent PDE of the form
∂ty −κ∆y = f,
(H.1)
with suitable initial and boundary conditions, using ROM. In particular we will use reduced basis
methods based on proper orthogonal decomposition. After this we will move on to solve an optimiza-
tion problem of the form
min
y,u

(H.2a)

(H.2b)
in
Σ = γ × (0, T),
(H.2c)
in
Q0 = Ω× {0}
(H.2d)
by using reduced order modeling (ROM). From hereon, we will try to incorporate neural networks and
replace some aspects of the numerical algorithms, used to solve (H.2), with neural networks.
Having successfully solved (H.1) and (H.2) we move on to nonlinear cases.
We start by looking
at PDE’s of the form
∂ty −κ∆y + b(y) = f,
(H.3)

142
Project Plan and Evaluation
with suitable initial and boundary conditions, where b is some nonlinear reaction term. Again, we will
solve this using ROM. Next, we will incorporate this into an optimization problem,
min

and solve this using ROM. Lastly we will, as in the linear case, replace aspects of the scientiﬁc com-
puting algorithms with neural networks. Everything will be programmed using Python
While solving the above mentioned problems, we will simultaneously develop the theory in these
three main areas
• Reduced Order Modeling.
• Optimal Control of PDEs.
• Neural Networks.
In general there will be spent most time developing the theory of ROM and all the theory will be put
into context of the problems (H.1)-(H.4).
To be Decided
Implementations
As of now, we plan to solve the high-ﬁdelity problems using the FEnICS framework in Python, then
program the ROM algorithms ourselves and use TensorFlow for the ANN implementations. However,
this is not fully decided since we are not sure whether this combination of frameworks makes sense all
the way through.
Theory
We will, at ﬁrst, put most focus onto the theory behind ROM. What is yet to be decided is how much
time and energy will be spent on developing the theory of optimal control and ANN.
Project Evaluation
We successfully addressed the project aim stated in the original plan and solved almost all the prob-
lems we set out plus some more. Instead of solving a linear control problem we jumped straight to
nonlinear control problems. Furthermore, we considered diﬀerent dynamic PDEs without control and
two nonlinear control problems as well the slightly more complicated case with constraints on the
control function and domain. For the neural networks we used PyTorch instead of TensorFlow, which
was stated in the project plan. Regarding the theory, we covered the three main areas as well as
necessary. However, personally I would have liked to have included more theory. That is, some more
==PAGE:157==
143
Figure H.1: Original time schedule made in the beginning of September.
proves and rigorous arguments.
Studying and writing the theory chapters took quite a while.
Before starting the project, I had
close to no knowledge about PDE-constrained optimization, reduced order models and neural net-
works. I did, however, have a solid background in scientiﬁc computing, optimization and functional
analysis and to some degree machine learning. This meant that there was a lot for me to learn along-
side the implementations and writing. Furthermore, Python was not a programming language I have
used a lot. Which meant that quite some time has been spent on moving from Matlab to Python.
The reason for this choice was 1) a personal interest of mine in getting to know th language and 2)
being able to utilize frameworks such as FEniCS and PyTorch. Conclusively, I would say that it has
been very interesting and that it worked out great, partially due to my already established foundation.
The time schedule was not followed very well. First of all it was made far too detailed for practi-
cal purposes. Secondly, it was impossible to foresee where the problems would arise. FEniCS caused
more troubles than expected. It is a great framework solving PDEs, but as soon as one wants to use
for more specialized purposes, it is often time consuming to ﬁgure out how to use FEniCS properly. In
this thesis we tried to use it for a space-time discretization without luck. Furthermore, we spent more
time than expected on generating the right matrices and including discrete vectors into the FEniCS
function framework.
Besides FEniCS, it took much more time than expected to program the SQP method. For around a
month I was stuck on the same problem without much progress. However, once we solve the problem
it went quite smooth from there.
On the other hand, PyTorch was easier to deal with than expected. Getting an overall framework up
and running for neural networks went faster than what was originally planned for. That being said,
the trial-and-error phase for the neural networkls was much longer than expected. It was diﬃcult to
decide on what exactly to look at how to improve the networks. Choosing two diﬀerent control prob-
lems with two set-ups each led to four diﬀerent cases to analyze and on top of that we also studied the
control constrained cases which led to four more. In hindsight, we should have focused our attention
on fewer set-ups.
Other problems arose when we had to deal with the DTU HPC systems. Firstly, there were problems
in running FEniCS and PyTorch on the same set-up. Secondly we had troubles formulating the right
bash scripts and so on. Lastly, w did not program an actual parallelization until the beginning of
January which was too late. If I were to start over, I would have put more focus on that part earlier
==PAGE:158==
144
Project Plan and Evaluation
and even spent time utilizing GPUs for network training. This would have made the trial-and-error
phase much more eﬃcient.
Regarding the writing process, I was quite at spreading it out during the whole semester which meant
that I was under large writing pressure in the end.
Actual Time Schedule
• September.
Most of the time was spent on getting FEniCS up and running alongside the
POD-Galerkin method for the heat equation and Burgers’ equation. In the beginning we spent
time on trying to solve the problems using a space-time galerkin method in FEniCS without luck
before settling on using the θ-method. Furthermore, much time was spent on learning about
reduced order models.
• October. This month was partially devoted to reﬁning the results from previous month as
well as documenting it and writing theory on reduced order models as well as implementing
the problem with Robin boundary conditions. The second half was spent on learning about
PDE-constrained optimization and implementing the SQP for Burgers equation.
• November.
The majority of the month was spent solving the optimal control problem for
Burgers’ equation, before moving on to the y3-equation, as well writing theory for reduced order
models.
In the last week I started to look at neural networks- Both the theory as well as
implementation once the SQP and POD-SQP method were implemnted successfully for both
Burgers’ equations and the y3-equation.
• December.
The majority of this month was dedicated to testing the neural networks and
getting the HPC framework to work in order to be able to generate data. Furthermore, we
implemented the control constrained case and wrote large chunks of the optimal control and
neural network chapters.
• January. The beginning of this month was spent on properly parallelizing the code such that
the networks could be trained on multiple CPUs. From thereon the ﬁnal results were produced
and documented alongside the report was being ﬁnished.
